10056{_}1601{_}BOOK XIX  Teachings of Various Chief Disciples   "The learned official," said Tsz-chang, "who when he sees danger ahead will risk his very life, who when he sees a chance of success is mindful of what is just and proper, who in his religious acts is mindful of the duty of reverence, and when in mourning thinks of his loss, is indeed a fit and proper person for his place."
10056{_}1602{_}Again he said, "If a person hold to virtue but never advance in it, and if he have faith in right principles and do not build himself up in them, how can he be regarded either as having such, or as being without them?"
10056{_}1603{_}Tsz-hia's disciples asked Tsz-chang his views about intercourse with others.
10056{_}1604{_}"What says your Master?"
10056{_}1605{_}he rejoined.
10056{_}1606{_}"He says," they replied, "'Associate with those who are qualified, and repel from you such as are not,'" Tsz-chang then said, "That is different from what I have learnt.
10056{_}1607{_}A superior man esteems the worthy and wise, and bears with all.
10056{_}1608{_}He makes much of the good and capable, and pities the incapable.
10056{_}1609{_}Am I eminently worthy and wise?--who is there then among men whom I will not bear with?
10056{_}1610{_}Am I not worthy and wise?--others will be minded to repel me: I have nothing to do with repelling them."
10056{_}1611{_}Sayings of Tsz-hia:--  "Even in inferior pursuits there must be something worthy of contemplation, but if carried to an extreme there is danger of fanaticism; hence the superior man does not engage in them.
10056{_}1612{_}"The student who daily recognizes how much he yet lacks, and as the months pass forgets not what he has succeeded in learning, may undoubtedly be called a lover of learning.
10056{_}1613{_}"Wide research and steadfast purpose, eager questioning and close reflection--all this tends to humanize a man.
10056{_}1614{_}"As workmen spend their time in their workshops for the perfecting of their work, so superior men apply their minds to study in order to make themselves thoroughly conversant with their subjects.
10056{_}1615{_}"When an inferior man does a wrong thing, he is sure to gloss it over.
10056{_}1616{_}"The superior man is seen in three different aspects:--look at him from a distance, he is imposing in appearance; approach him, he is gentle and warm-hearted; hear him speak, he is acute and strict.
10056{_}1617{_}"Let such a man have the people's confidence, and he will get much work out of them; so long, however, as he does not possess their confidence they will regard him as grinding them down.
10056{_}1618{_}"When confidence is reposed in him, he may then with impunity administer reproof; so long as it is not, he will be regarded as a detractor.
10056{_}1619{_}"Where there is no over-stepping of barriers in the practice of the higher virtues, there may be freedom to pass in and out in the practice of the lower ones."
10056{_}1620{_}Tsz-yu had said, "The pupils in the school of Tsz-hia are good enough at such things as sprinkling and scrubbing floors, answering calls and replying to questions from superiors, and advancing and retiring to and from such; but these things are only offshoots--as to the root of things they are nowhere.
10056{_}1621{_}What is the use of all that?"
10056{_}1622{_}When this came to the ears of Tsz-hia, he said, "Ah!
10056{_}1623{_}there he is mistaken.
10056{_}1624{_}What does a master, in his methods of teaching, consider first in his precepts?
10056{_}1625{_}And what does he account next, as that about which he may be indifferent?
10056{_}1626{_}It is like as in the study of plants--classification by _differentiae_.
10056{_}1627{_}How may a master play fast and loose in his methods of instruction?
10056{_}1628{_}Would they not indeed be sages, who could take in at once the first principles and the final developments of things?"
10056{_}1629{_}Further observations of Tsz-hia:--  "In the public service devote what energy and time remain to study.
10056{_}1630{_}After study devote what energy and time remain to the public service.
10056{_}1631{_}"As to the duties of mourning, let them cease when the grief is past.
10056{_}1632{_}"My friend Tsz-chang, although he has the ability to tackle hard things, has not yet the virtue of philanthropy."
10056{_}1633{_}The learned Tsang observed, "How loftily Tsz-chang bears himself!
10056{_}1634{_}Difficult indeed along with him to practise philanthropy!"
10056{_}1635{_}Again he said, "I have heard this said by the Master, that 'though men may not exert themselves to the utmost in other duties, yet surely in the duty of mourning for their parents they will do so!'"
10056{_}1636{_}Again, "This also I have heard said by the Master: 'The filial piety of Mang Chwang in other respects might be equalled, but as manifested in his making no changes among his father's ministers, nor in his father's mode of government--that aspect of it could not easily be equalled.'"
10056{_}1637{_}Yang Fu, having been made senior Criminal Judge by the Chief of the Mang clan, consulted with the learned Tsang.
10056{_}1638{_}The latter advised him as follows: "For a long time the Chiefs have failed in their government, and the people have become unsettled.
10056{_}1639{_}When you arrive at the facts of their cases, do not rejoice at your success in that, but rather be sorry for them, and have pity upon them."
10056{_}1640{_}Tsz-kung once observed, "We speak of 'the iniquity of Chau'--but 'twas not so great as this.
10056{_}1641{_}And so it is that the superior man is averse from settling in this sink, into which everything runs that is foul in the empire."
10056{_}1642{_}Again he said, "Faults in a superior man are like eclipses of the sun or moon: when he is guilty of a trespass men all see it; and when he is himself again, all look up to him."
10056{_}1643{_}Kung-sun Ch'an of Wei inquired of Tsz-kung how Confucius acquired his learning.
10056{_}1644{_}Tsz-kung replied, "The teachings of Wan and Wu have not yet fallen to the ground.
10056{_}1645{_}They exist in men.
10056{_}1646{_}Worthy and wise men have the more important of these stored up in their minds; and others, who are not such, store up the less important of them; and as no one is thus without the teachings of Wan and Wu, how should our Master not have learned?
10056{_}1647{_}And moreover what permanent preceptor could he have?"
10056{_}1648{_}Shuh-sun Wu-shuh, addressing the high officials at the Court, remarked that Tsz-kung was a greater worthy than Confucius.
10056{_}1649{_}Tsz-fuh King-pih went and informed Tsz-kung of this remark.
10056{_}1650{_}Tsz-kung said, "Take by way of comparison the walls outside our houses.
10056{_}1651{_}My wall is shoulder-high, and you may look over it and see what the house and its contents are worth.
10056{_}1652{_}My Master's wall is tens of feet high, and unless you should effect an entrance by the door, you would fail to behold the beauty of the ancestral hall and the rich array of all its officers.
10056{_}1653{_}And they who effect an entrance by the door, methinks, are few!
10056{_}1654{_}Was it not, however, just like him--that remark of the Chief?"
10056{_}1655{_}Shuh-sun Wu-shuh had been casting a slur on the character of Confucius.
10056{_}1656{_}"No use doing that," said Tsz-kung; "he is irreproachable.
10056{_}1657{_}The wisdom and worth of other men are little hills and mounds of earth: traversible.
10056{_}1658{_}He is the sun, or the moon, impossible to reach and pass.
10056{_}1659{_}And what harm, I ask, can a man do to the sun or the moon, by wishing to intercept himself from either?
10056{_}1660{_}It all shows that he knows not how to gauge capacity."
10056{_}1661{_}Tsz-k'in, addressing Tsz-kung, said, "You depreciate yourself.
10056{_}1662{_}Confucius is surely not a greater worthy than yourself."
10056{_}1663{_}Tsz-kung replied, "In the use of words one ought never to be incautious; because a gentleman for one single utterance of his is apt to be considered a wise man, and for a single utterance may be accounted unwise.
10056{_}1664{_}No more might one think of attaining to the Master's perfections than think of going upstairs to Heaven!
10056{_}1665{_}Were it ever his fortune to be at the head of the government of a country, then that which is spoken of as 'establishing the country' would be establishment indeed; he would be its guide and it would follow him, he would tranquillize it and it would render its willing homage: he would give forward impulses to it to which it would harmoniously respond.
10056{_}1666{_}In his life he would be its glory, at his death there would be great lamentation.
10056{_}1667{_}How indeed could such as he be equalled?"
10056{_}1668{_}BOOK XX  Extracts from the Book of History   The Emperor Yau said to Shun, "Ah, upon you, upon your person, lies the Heaven-appointed order of succession!
10056{_}1669{_}Faithfully hold to it, without any deflection; for if within the four seas necessity and want befall the people, your own revenue will forever come to an end."
10056{_}1670{_}Shun also used the same language in handing down the appointment to Yu.
10056{_}1671{_}The Emperor T'ang in his prayer, said, "I, the child Li, presume to avail me of an ox of dusky hue, and presume to manifestly announce to Thee, O God, the most high and Sovereign Potentate, that to the transgressor I dare not grant forgiveness, nor yet keep in abeyance Thy ministers.
10056{_}1672{_}Judgment rests in Thine heart, O God.
10056{_}1673{_}Should we ourself transgress, may the guilt not be visited everywhere upon all.
10056{_}1674{_}Should the people all transgress, be the guilt upon ourself!"
10056{_}1675{_}Chow possessed great gifts, by which the able and good were richly endowed.
10056{_}1676{_}"Although," said King Wu, "he is surrounded by his near relatives, they are not to be compared with men of humane spirit.
10056{_}1677{_}The people are suffering wrongs, and the remedy rests with me--the one man."
10056{_}1678{_}After Wu had given diligent attention to the various weights and measures, examined the laws and regulations, and restored the degraded officials, good government everywhere ensued.
10056{_}1679{_}He caused ruined States to flourish again, reinstated intercepted heirs, and promoted to office men who had gone into retirement; and the hearts of the people throughout the empire drew towards him.
10056{_}1680{_}Among matters of prime consideration with him were these--food for the people, the duty of mourning, and sacrificial offerings to the departed.
10056{_}1681{_}He was liberal and large-hearted, and so won all hearts; true, and so was trusted by the people; energetic, and thus became a man of great achievements; just in his rule, and all were well content.
10056{_}1682{_}Tsz-chang in a conversation with Confucius asked, "What say you is essential for the proper conduct of government?"
10056{_}1683{_}The Master replied, "Let the ruler hold in high estimation the five excellences, and eschew the four evils; then may he conduct his government properly."
10056{_}1684{_}"And what call you the five excellences?"
10056{_}1685{_}he was asked.
10056{_}1686{_}"They are," he said, "Bounty without extravagance; burdening without exciting discontent; desire without covetousness; dignity without haughtiness; show of majesty without fierceness."
10056{_}1687{_}"What mean you," asked Tsz-chang, "by bounty without extravagance?"
10056{_}1688{_}"Is it not this," he replied--"to make that which is of benefit to the people still more beneficial?
10056{_}1689{_}When he selects for them such labors as it is possible for them to do, and exacts them, who will then complain?
10056{_}1690{_}So when his desire is the virtue of humaneness, and he attains it, how shall he then be covetous?
10056{_}1691{_}And if--whether he have to do with few or with many, with small or with great--he do not venture ever to be careless, is not this also to have dignity without haughtiness?
10056{_}1692{_}And if--when properly vested in robe and cap, and showing dignity in his every look--his appearance be so imposing that the people look up to and stand in awe of him, is not this moreover to show majesty without fierceness?"
10056{_}1693{_}"What, then, do you call the four evils?"
10056{_}1694{_}said Tsz-chang.
10056{_}1695{_}The answer here was, "Omitting to instruct the people and then inflicting capital punishment on them--which means cruel tyranny.
10056{_}1696{_}Omitting to give them warning and yet looking for perfection in them--which means oppression.
10056{_}1697{_}Being slow and late in issuing requisitions, and exacting strict punctuality in the returns--which means robbery.
10056{_}1698{_}And likewise, in intercourse with men, to expend and to receive in a stingy manner--which is to act the part of a mere commissioner."
10056{_}1699{_}"None can be a superior man," said the Master, "who does not recognize the decrees of Heaven.
10056{_}1700{_}"None can have stability in him without a knowledge of the proprieties.
10056{_}1701{_}"None can know a man without knowing his utterances."
10056{_}1702{_}THE SAYINGS OF MENICUS  [Translated into English by James Legge_]    INTRODUCTION   A hundred years after the time of Confucius the Chinese nation seemed to have fallen back into their original condition of lawlessness and oppression.
10056{_}1703{_}The King's power and authority was laughed to scorn, the people were pillaged by the feudal nobility, and famine reigned in many districts.
10056{_}1704{_}The foundations of truth and social order seemed to be overthrown.
10056{_}1705{_}There were teachers of immorality abroad, who published the old Epicurean doctrine, "Let us eat and drink, for to-morrow we die."
10056{_}1706{_}This teaching was accompanied by a spirit of cold-blooded egotism which extinguished every spark of Confucian altruism.
10056{_}1707{_}Even the pretended disciples of Confucius confused the precepts of the Master, and by stripping them of their narrow significance rendered them nugatory.
10056{_}1708{_}It was at this point that Mang-tsze, "Mang the philosopher," arose.
10056{_}1709{_}He was sturdy in bodily frame, vigorous in mind, profound in political sagacity and utterly fearless in denouncing the errors of his countrymen.
10056{_}1710{_}He had been brought up among the disciples of Confucius, in whose province he was born B.C.
10056{_}1711{_}372, but he was much more active and aggressive, less a Mystic than a fanatic, in comparison!
10056{_}1712{_}with his Master.
10056{_}1713{_}He resolved on active measures in stemming the tendency of his day.
10056{_}1714{_}He did indeed surround himself with a school of disciples, but instead of making a series of desultory travels, teaching in remote places and along the high-road, he went to the heart of the evil.
10056{_}1715{_}He presented himself like a second John the Baptist at the courts of kings and princes, and there boldly denounced vice and misrule.
10056{_}1716{_}It was not difficult for a Chinese scholar and teacher to find access to the highest of the land.
10056{_}1717{_}The Chinese believed in the divine right of learning, just as they believed in the divine right of kings.
10056{_}1718{_}Mang employed every weapon of persuasion in trying to combat heresy and oppression; alternately ridiculing and reproving: now appealing in a burst of moral enthusiasm, and now denouncing in terms of cutting sarcasm the abuses which after all he failed to check.
10056{_}1719{_}The last prince whom he successfully confronted was the Marquis of Lu, who turned him carelessly away.
10056{_}1720{_}He accepted this as the Divine sentence of his failure, "That I have not found in this marquis, a ruler who would hearken to me is an intimation of heaven."
10056{_}1721{_}Henceforth he lived in retirement until his ninety-seventh year; but from his apparent failure sprang a practical success.
10056{_}1722{_}His written teachings are amongst the most lively and epigrammatic works of Chinese literature, have done much to keep alive amongst his countrymen the spirit of Confucianism, and even Western readers may drink wisdom from this spring of Oriental lore.
10056{_}1723{_}The following selections from his sayings well exhibit the spirit of his system of philosophy and morality.
10056{_}1724{_}E.W.
10056{_}1725{_}THE SAYINGS OF MENCIUS    BOOK I  KING HWUY OF LEANG   Part I  Mencius went to see King Hwuy of Leang.
10056{_}1726{_}[1] The king said, "Venerable Sir, since you have not counted it far to come here a distance of a thousand li, may I presume that you are likewise provided with counsels to profit my kingdom?"
10056{_}1727{_}Mencius replied, "Why must your Majesty used that word 'profit'?
10056{_}1728{_}What I am likewise provided with are counsels to benevolence and righteousness; and these are my only topics.
10056{_}1729{_}"If your Majesty say, 'What is to be done to profit my kingdom?'
10056{_}1730{_}the great officers will say, 'What is to be done to profit our families?'
10056{_}1731{_}and the inferior officers and the common people will say, 'What is to be done to profit our persons?'
10056{_}1732{_}Superiors and inferiors will try to take the profit the one from the other, and the kingdom will be endangered.
10056{_}1733{_}In the kingdom of ten thousand chariots, the murderer of his ruler will be the chief of a family of a thousand chariots.
10056{_}1734{_}In the State of a thousand chariots, the murderer of his ruler will be the chief of a family of a hundred chariots.
10056{_}1735{_}To have a thousand in ten thousand, and a hundred in a thousand, cannot be regarded as not a large allowance; but if righteousness be put last and profit first, they will not be satisfied without snatching all.
10056{_}1736{_}"There never was a man trained to benevolence who neglected his parents.
10056{_}1737{_}There never was a man trained to righteousness who made his ruler an after consideration.
10056{_}1738{_}Let your Majesty likewise make benevolence and righteousness your only themes--Why must you speak of profit?"
10056{_}1739{_}When Mencius, another day, was seeing King Hwuy of Leang, the King went and stood with him by a pond, and, looking round on the wild geese and deer, large and small, said, "Do wise and good princes also take pleasure in these things?"
10056{_}1740{_}Mencius replied, "Being wise and good, they then have pleasure in these things.
10056{_}1741{_}If they are not wise and good, though they have these things, they do not find pleasure."
10056{_}1742{_}It is said in the 'Book of Poetry':--    'When he planned the commencement of the Marvellous tower,   He planned it, and defined it,   And the people in crowds undertook the work,   And in no time completed it.
10056{_}1743{_}When he planned the commencement, he said, "Be not in a hurry."
10056{_}1744{_}But the people came as if they were his children.
10056{_}1745{_}The king was in the Marvellous park,   Where the does were lying down--   The does so sleek and fat;   With the white birds glistening.
10056{_}1746{_}The king was by the Marvellous pond;--   How full was it of fishes leaping about!'
10056{_}1747{_}King Wan used the strength of the people to make his tower and pond, and the people rejoiced to do the work, calling the tower 'the Marvellous Tower,' and the pond 'the Marvellous Pond,' and being glad that he had his deer, his fishes and turtles.
10056{_}1748{_}The ancients caused their people to have pleasure as well as themselves, and therefore they could enjoy it.
10056{_}1749{_}"In the Declaration of T'ang it is said, 'O Sun, when wilt thou expire?
10056{_}1750{_}We will die together with thee.'
10056{_}1751{_}The people wished for Keeh's death, though they should die with him.
10056{_}1752{_}Although he had his tower, his pond, birds and animals, how could he have pleasure alone?"
10056{_}1753{_}King Hwuy of Leang said, "Small as my virtue is, in the government of my kingdom, I do indeed exert my mind to the utmost.
10056{_}1754{_}If the year be bad inside the Ho, I remove as many of the people as I can to the east of it, and convey grain to the country inside.
10056{_}1755{_}If the year be bad on the east of the river, I act on the same plan.
10056{_}1756{_}On examining the governmental methods of the neighboring kingdoms, I do not find there is any ruler who exerts his mind as I do.
10056{_}1757{_}And yet the people of the neighboring kings do not decrease, nor do my people increase--how is this?"
10056{_}1758{_}Mencius replied, "Your Majesty loves war; allow me to take an illustration from war.
10056{_}1759{_}The soldiers move forward at the sound of the drum; and when the edges of their weapons have been crossed, on one side, they throw away their buff coats, trail their weapons behind them, and run.
10056{_}1760{_}Some run a hundred paces and then stop; some run fifty paces and stop.
10056{_}1761{_}What would you think if these, because they had run but fifty paces, should laugh at those who ran a hundred paces?"
10056{_}1762{_}The king said, "They cannot do so.
10056{_}1763{_}They only did not run a hundred paces; but they also ran."
10056{_}1764{_}Mencius said, "Since your Majesty knows this you have no ground to expect that your people will become more numerous than those of the neighboring kingdoms.
10056{_}1765{_}"If the seasons of husbandry be not interfered with, the grain will be more than can be eaten.
10056{_}1766{_}If close nets are not allowed to enter the pools and ponds, the fish and turtles will be more than can be consumed.
10056{_}1767{_}If the axes and bills enter the hill-forests only at the proper times, the wood will be more than can be used.
10056{_}1768{_}When the grain and fish and turtles are more than can be eaten, and there is more wood than can be used, this enables the people to nourish their living and do all offices for their dead, without any feeling against any.
10056{_}1769{_}But this condition, in which the people nourish their living, and do all offices to their dead without having any feeling against any, is the first step in the Royal way.
10056{_}1770{_}"Let mulberry trees be planted about the homesteads with their five acres, and persons of fifty years will be able to wear silk.
10056{_}1771{_}In keeping fowls, pigs, dogs, and swine, let not their time of breeding be neglected, and persons of seventy years will be able to eat flesh.
10056{_}1772{_}Let there not be taken away the time that is proper for the cultivation of the field allotment of a hundred acres, and the family of several mouths will not suffer from hunger.
10056{_}1773{_}Let careful attention be paid to the teaching in the various schools, with repeated inculcation of the filial and fraternal duties, and gray-haired men will not be seen upon the roads, carrying burdens on their backs or on their heads.
10056{_}1774{_}It has never been that the ruler of a State where these results were seen, persons of seventy wearing silk and eating flesh, and the black-haired people suffering neither from hunger nor cold, did not attain to the Royal dignity.
10056{_}1775{_}"Your dogs and swine eat the food of men, and you do not know to store up of the abundance.
10056{_}1776{_}There are people dying from famine on the roads, and you do not know to issue your stores for their relief.
10056{_}1777{_}When men die, you say, 'It is not owing to me; it is owing to the year,' In what does this differ from stabbing a man and killing him, and then saying, 'It was not I; it was the weapon'?
10056{_}1778{_}Let your Majesty cease to lay the blame on the year and instantly the people, all under the sky, will come to you."
10056{_}1779{_}King Hwuy of Leang said, "I wish quietly to receive your instructions."
10056{_}1780{_}Mencius replied, "Is there any difference between killing a man with a stick and with a sword?"
10056{_}1781{_}"There is no difference," was the answer.
10056{_}1782{_}Mencius continued, "Is there any difference between doing it with a sword and with governmental measures?"
10056{_}1783{_}"There is not," was the answer again.
10056{_}1784{_}Mencius then said, "In your stalls there are fat beasts; in your stables there are fat horses.
10056{_}1785{_}But your people have the look of hunger, and in the fields there are those who have died of famine.
10056{_}1786{_}This is leading on beasts to devour men.
10056{_}1787{_}Beasts devour one another, and men hate them for doing so.
10056{_}1788{_}When he who is called the parent of the people conducts his government so as to be chargeable with leading on beasts to devour men, where is that parental relation to the people?
10056{_}1789{_}Chung-ne said, 'Was he not without posterity who first made wooden images to bury with the dead?'
10056{_}1790{_}So he said, because that man made the semblances of men and used them for that purpose; what shall be thought of him who causes his people to die of hunger?"
10056{_}1791{_}King Hwuy of Leang said, "There was not in the kingdom a stronger State than Ts'in, as you, venerable Sir, know.
10056{_}1792{_}But since it descended to me, on the east we were defeated by Ts'e, and then my eldest son perished; on the west we lost seven hundred li of territory to Ts'in; and on the south we have sustained disgrace at the hands of Ts'oo.
10056{_}1793{_}I have brought shame on my departed predecessors, and wish on their account to wipe it away once for all.
10056{_}1794{_}What course is to be pursued to accomplish this?"
10056{_}1795{_}Mencius replied, "With a territory only a hundred li square it has been possible to obtain the Royal dignity.
10056{_}1796{_}If your Majesty will indeed dispense a benevolent government to the people, being sparing in the use of punishments and fines, and making the taxes and levies of produce light, so causing that the fields shall be ploughed deep, and the weeding well attended to, and that the able-bodied, during their days of leisure, shall cultivate their filial piety, fraternal duty, faithfulness, and truth, serving thereby, at home, their fathers and elder brothers, and, abroad, their elders and superiors, you will then have a people who can be employed with sticks which they have prepared to oppose the strong buff-coats and sharp weapons of the troops of Ts'in and Ts'oo.
10056{_}1797{_}"The rulers of those States rob their people of their time, so that they cannot plough and weed their fields in order to support their parents.
10056{_}1798{_}Parents suffer from cold and hunger; elder and younger brothers, wives and children, are separated and scattered abroad.
10056{_}1799{_}Those rulers drive their people into pitfalls or into the water; and your Majesty will go to punish them.
10056{_}1800{_}In such a case, who will oppose your Majesty?
10056{_}1801{_}In accordance with this is the saying, 'The benevolent has no enemy!'
10056{_}1802{_}I beg your Majesty not to doubt what I said."
10056{_}1803{_}Mencius had an interview with King Seang[2] of Leang.
10056{_}1804{_}When he came out he said to some persons, "When I looked at him from a distance, he did not appear like a ruler; when I drew near to him, I saw nothing venerable about him.
10056{_}1805{_}Abruptly he asked me, 'How can the kingdom, all under the sky, be settled?'
10056{_}1806{_}I replied, 'It will be settled by being united under one sway,'  "'Who can so unite it?'
10056{_}1807{_}he asked.
10056{_}1808{_}"I replied, 'He who has no pleasure in killing men can so unite it.'
10056{_}1809{_}"'Who can give it to him?'
10056{_}1810{_}he asked.
10056{_}1811{_}"I replied, 'All under heaven will give it to him.
10056{_}1812{_}Does your Majesty know the way of the growing grain?
10056{_}1813{_}During the seventh and eighth months, when drought prevails, the plants become dry.
10056{_}1814{_}Then the clouds collect densely in the heavens, and send down torrents of rain, so that the grain erects itself as if by a shoot.
10056{_}1815{_}When it does so, who can keep it back?
10056{_}1816{_}Now among those who are shepherds of men throughout the kingdom, there is not one who does not find pleasure in killing men.
10056{_}1817{_}If there were one who did not find pleasure in killing men, all the people under the sky would be looking towards him with outstretched necks.
10056{_}1818{_}Such being indeed the case, the people would go to him as water flows downwards with a rush, which no one can repress."
10056{_}1819{_}King Seuen of Ts'e asked, saying, "May I be informed by you of the transactions of Hwan of Ts'e and Wan of Ts'in?"
10056{_}1820{_}Mencius replied, "There were none of the disciples of Chung-ne who spoke about the affairs of Hwan and Wan, and therefore they have not been transmitted to these after-ages; your servant has not heard of them.
10056{_}1821{_}If you will have me speak, let it be about the principles of attaining to the Royal sway."
10056{_}1822{_}The king said, "Of what kind must his virtue be who can attain to the Royal sway?"
10056{_}1823{_}Mencius said, "If he loves and protects the people, it is impossible to prevent him from attaining it."
10056{_}1824{_}The king said, "Is such an one as poor I competent to love and protect the people?"
10056{_}1825{_}"Yes," was the reply.
10056{_}1826{_}"From what do you know that I am competent to that?"
10056{_}1827{_}"I have heard," said Mencius, "from Hoo Heih the following incident:--'The king,' said he, 'was sitting aloft in the hall, when some people appeared leading a bull past below it.
10056{_}1828{_}The king saw it, and asked where the bull was going, and being answered that they were going to consecrate a bell with its blood, he said, "Let it go, I cannot bear its frightened appearance--as if it were an innocent person going to the place of death."
10056{_}1829{_}They asked in reply whether, if they did so, they should omit the consecration of the bell, but the king said, "How can that be omitted?
10056{_}1830{_}Change it for a sheep."'
10056{_}1831{_}I do not know whether this incident occurred."
10056{_}1832{_}"It did," said the king, and Mencius replied, "The heart seen in this is sufficient to carry you to the Royal sway.
10056{_}1833{_}The people all supposed that your Majesty grudged the animal, but your servant knows surely that it was your Majesty's not being able to bear the sight of the creature's distress which made you do as you did."
10056{_}1834{_}The king said, "You are right; and yet there really was an appearance of what the people imagined.
10056{_}1835{_}But though Ts'e be narrow and small, how should I grudge a bull?
10056{_}1836{_}Indeed it was because I could not bear its frightened appearance, as if it were an innocent person going to the place of death, that therefore I changed it for a sheep."
10056{_}1837{_}Mencius said, "Let not your Majesty deem it strange that the people should think you grudged the animal.
10056{_}1838{_}When you changed a large one for a small, how should they know the true reason?
10056{_}1839{_}If you felt pained by its being led without any guilt to the place of death, what was there to choose between a bull and a sheep?"
10056{_}1840{_}The king laughed and said, "What really was my mind in the matter?
10056{_}1841{_}I did not grudge the value of the bull, and yet I changed it for a sheep!
10056{_}1842{_}There was reason in the people's saying that I grudged the creature."
10056{_}1843{_}Mencius said, "There is no harm in their saying so.
10056{_}1844{_}It was an artifice of benevolence.
10056{_}1845{_}You saw the bull, and had not seen the sheep.
10056{_}1846{_}So is the superior man affected towards animals, that, having seen them alive, he cannot bear to see them die, and, having heard their dying cries, he cannot bear to eat their flesh.
10056{_}1847{_}On this account he keeps away from his stalls and kitchen."
10056{_}1848{_}The king was pleased and said, "The Ode says,    'What other men have in their minds,   I can measure by reflection,'  This might be spoken of you, my Master.
10056{_}1849{_}I indeed did the thing, but when I turned my thoughts inward and sought for it, I could not discover my own mind.
10056{_}1850{_}When you, Master, spoke those words, the movements of compassion began to work in my mind.
10056{_}1851{_}But how is it that this heart has in it what is equal to the attainment of the Royal sway?"
10056{_}1852{_}Mencius said, "Suppose a man were to make this statement to your Majesty, 'My strength is sufficient to lift three thousand catties, but is not sufficient to lift one feather; my eyesight is sharp enough to examine the point of an autumn hair, but I do not see a wagon-load of fagots,' would your Majesty allow what he said?"
10056{_}1853{_}"No," was the king's remark, and Mencius proceeded, "Now here is kindness sufficient to reach to animals, and yet no benefits are extended from it to the people--how is this?
10056{_}1854{_}is an exception to be made here?
10056{_}1855{_}The truth is, the feather's not being lifted is because the strength was not used; the wagon-load of firewood's not being seen is because the eyesight was not used; and the people's not being loved and protected is because the kindness is not used.
10056{_}1856{_}Therefore your Majesty's not attaining to the Royal sway is because you do not do it, and not because you are not able to do it."
10056{_}1857{_}The king asked, "How may the difference between him who does not do a thing and him who is not able to do it be graphically set forth?"
10056{_}1858{_}Mencius replied, "In such a thing as taking the T'ae mountain under your arm, and leaping with it over the North Sea, if you say to people, 'I am not able to do it,' that is a real case of not being able.
10056{_}1859{_}In such a matter as breaking off a branch from a tree at the order of a superior, if you say to people, 'I am not able to do it,' it is not a case of not being able to do it.
10056{_}1860{_}And so your Majesty's not attaining to the Royal sway is not such a case as that of taking the T'ae mountain under your arm and leaping over the North Sea with it; but it is a case like that of breaking off a branch from a tree.
10056{_}1861{_}"Treat with reverence due to age the elders in your own family, so that those in the families of others shall be similarly treated; treat with the kindness due to youth the young in your own family, so that those in the families of others shall be similarly treated--do this and the kingdom may be made to go round in your palm.
10056{_}1862{_}It is said in the 'Book of Poetry,'    'His example acted on his wife,   Extended to his brethren,   And was felt by all the clans and States;'  Telling us how King Wan simply took this kindly heart, and exercised it towards those parties.
10056{_}1863{_}Therefore the carrying out of the feeling of kindness by a ruler will suffice for the love and protection of all within the four seas; and if he do not carry it out, he will not be able to protect his wife and children.
10056{_}1864{_}The way in which the ancients came greatly to surpass other men was no other than this, that they carried out well what they did, so as to affect others.
10056{_}1865{_}Now your kindness is sufficient to reach to animals, and yet no benefits are extended from it to the people.
10056{_}1866{_}How is this?
10056{_}1867{_}Is an exception to be made here?
10056{_}1868{_}"By weighing we know what things are light, and what heavy.
10056{_}1869{_}By measuring we know what things are long, and what short.
10056{_}1870{_}All things are so dealt with, and the mind requires specially to be so.
10056{_}1871{_}I beg your Majesty to measure it.--Your Majesty collects your equipments of war, endangers your soldiers and officers and excites the resentment of the various princes--do these things cause you pleasure in your mind?"
10056{_}1872{_}The king said, "No.
10056{_}1873{_}How should I derive pleasure from these things?
10056{_}1874{_}My object in them is to seek for what I greatly desire."
10056{_}1875{_}Mencius said, "May I hear from you what it is that your Majesty greatly desires?"
10056{_}1876{_}The king laughed, and did not speak.
10056{_}1877{_}Mencius resumed, "Are you led to desire it because you have not enough of rich and sweet food for your mouth?
10056{_}1878{_}or because you have not enough of light and warm clothing for your body?
10056{_}1879{_}or because you have not enough of beautifully colored objects to satisfy your eyes?
10056{_}1880{_}or because there are not voices and sounds enough to fill your ears?
10056{_}1881{_}or because you have not enough of attendants and favorites to stand before you and receive your orders?
10056{_}1882{_}Your Majesty's various officers are sufficient to supply you with all these things.
10056{_}1883{_}How can your Majesty have such a desire on account of them?"
10056{_}1884{_}"No," said the king, "my desire is not on account of them."
10056{_}1885{_}Mencius observed, "Then what your Majesty greatly desires can be known.
10056{_}1886{_}You desire to enlarge your territories, to have Ts'in and Ts'oo coming to your court, to rule the Middle States, and to attract to you the barbarous tribes that surround them.
10056{_}1887{_}But to do what you do in order to seek for what you desire is like climbing a tree to seek for fish."
10056{_}1888{_}"Is it so bad as that?"
10056{_}1889{_}said the king.
10056{_}1890{_}"I apprehend it is worse," was the reply.
10056{_}1891{_}"If you climb a tree to seek for fish, although you do not get the fish, you have no subsequent calamity.
10056{_}1892{_}But if you do what you do in order to seek for what you desire, doing it even with all your heart, you will assuredly afterwards meet with calamities."
10056{_}1893{_}The king said, "May I hear what they will be?"
10056{_}1894{_}Mencius replied, "If the people of Tsow were fighting with the people of Ts'oo, which of them does your Majesty think would conquer?"
10056{_}1895{_}"The people of Ts'oo would conquer," was the answer, and Mencius pursued, "So then, a small State cannot contend with a great, few cannot contend with many, nor can the weak contend with the strong.
10056{_}1896{_}The territory within the seas would embrace nine divisions, each of a thousand li square.
10056{_}1897{_}All Ts'e together is one of them.
10056{_}1898{_}If with one part you try to subdue the other eight, what is the difference between that and Tsow's contending with Ts'oo?
10056{_}1899{_}With the desire which you have, you must turn back to the proper course for its attainment.
10056{_}1900{_}"Now, if your Majesty will institute a government whose action shall all be benevolent, this will cause all the officers in the kingdom to wish to stand in your Majesty's court, the farmers all to wish to plough in your Majesty's fields, the merchants, both travelling and stationary, all to wish to store their goods in your Majesty's market-places, travellers and visitors all to wish to travel on your Majesty's roads, and all under heaven who feel aggrieved by their rulers to wish to come and complain to your Majesty.
10056{_}1901{_}When they are so bent, who will be able to keep them back?"
10056{_}1902{_}The king said, "I am stupid and cannot advance to this.
10056{_}1903{_}But I wish you, my Master, to assist my intentions.
10056{_}1904{_}Teach me clearly, and although I am deficient in intelligence and vigor, I should like to try at least to institute such a government."
10056{_}1905{_}Mencius replied, "They are only men of education, who, without a certain livelihood, are able to maintain a fixed heart.
10056{_}1906{_}As to the people, if they have not a certain livelihood, they will be found not to have a fixed heart.
10056{_}1907{_}And if they have not a fixed heart, there is nothing which they will not do in the way of self-abandonment, of moral deflection, of depravity, and of wild license.
10056{_}1908{_}When they have thus been involved in crime, to follow them up and punish them, is to entrap the people.
10056{_}1909{_}How can such a thing as entrapping the people be done under the rule of a benevolent man?"
10056{_}1910{_}"Therefore, an intelligent ruler will regulate the livelihood of the people, so as to make sure that, above, they shall have sufficient wherewith to serve their parents, and below, sufficient wherewith to support their wives and children; that in good years they shall always be abundantly satisfied, and that in bad years they shall not be in danger of perishing.
10056{_}1911{_}After this he may urge them, and they will proceed to what is good, for in this case the people will follow after that with readiness.
10056{_}1912{_}"But now the livelihood of the people is so regulated, that, above, they have not sufficient wherewith to serve their parents, and, below, they have not sufficient wherewith to support their wives and children; even in good years their lives are always embittered, and in bad years they are in danger of perishing.
10056{_}1913{_}In such circumstances their only object is to escape from death, and they are afraid they will not succeed in doing so--what leisure have they to cultivate propriety and righteousness?
10056{_}1914{_}"If your Majesty wishes to carry out a benevolent government, why not turn back to what is the essential step to its attainment?
10056{_}1915{_}"Let mulberry trees be planted about the homesteads with their five acres, and persons of fifty years will be able to wear silk.
10056{_}1916{_}In keeping fowls, pigs, dogs, and swine, let not their times of breeding be neglected, and persons of seventy years will be able to eat flesh.
10056{_}1917{_}Let there not be taken away the time that is proper for the cultivation of the field-allotment of a hundred acres, and the family of eight mouths will not suffer from hunger.
10056{_}1918{_}Let careful attention be paid to the teaching in the various schools, with repeated inculcation of the filial and fraternal duties, and gray-haired men will not be seen upon the roads, carrying burdens on their backs or on their heads.
10056{_}1919{_}It has never been that the ruler of a State, where these results were seen, the old wearing silk and eating flesh, and the black-haired people suffering neither from hunger nor cold, did not attain to the Royal dignity."
10056{_}1920{_}[NOTE: _Books II, III, and IV are omitted_]   [Footnote 1: The title of this book in Chinese is--"King Hwuy of Leang; in chapters and sentences."
10056{_}1921{_}Like the Books of the Confucian Analects, those of this work are headed by two or three words at or near the commencement of them.
10056{_}1922{_}Each Book is divided into two parts.
10056{_}1923{_}This arrangement was made by Chaou K'e, and to him are due also the divisions into chapters, and sentences, or paragraphs, containing, it may be, many sentences.]
10056{_}1924{_}[Footnote 2: Seang was the son of King Hwuy.
10056{_}1925{_}The first year of his reign is supposed to be B.C.
10056{_}1926{_}317.
10056{_}1927{_}Seang's name was Hih.
10056{_}1928{_}As a posthumous epithet, Seang has various meanings: "Land-enlarger and Virtuous"; "Successful in Arms."
10056{_}1929{_}The interview here recorded seems to have taken place immediately after Hih's accession, and Mencius, it is said, was so disappointed by it that he soon after left the country.]
10056{_}1930{_}THE SHI-KING   [_Metrical translation by James Legge_]    INTRODUCTION   The wisdom of Confucius as a social reformer, as a teacher and guide of the Chinese people, is shown in many ways.
10056{_}1931{_}He not only gave them a code of personal deportment, providing them with rules for the etiquette and ceremony of life, but he instilled into them that profound spirit of domestic piety which is one of the strongest features in the Chinese character.
10056{_}1932{_}He took measures to secure also the intellectual cultivation of his followers, and his Five Canons contain all the most ancient works of Chinese literature, in the departments of poetry, history, philosophy, and legislation.
10056{_}1933{_}The Shi-King is a collection of Chinese poetry made by Confucius himself.
10056{_}1934{_}This great anthology consists of more than three hundred pieces, covering the whole range of Chinese lyric poetry, the oldest of which dates some eighteen centuries before Christ, while the latest of the selections must have been written at the beginning of the sixth century before Christ.
10056{_}1935{_}These poems are of the highest interest, and even nowadays may be read with delight by Europeans.
10056{_}1936{_}The ballad and the hymn are among the earliest forms of national poetry, and the contents of the Shi-King naturally show specimens of lyric poetry of this sort.
10056{_}1937{_}We find there not only hymns, but also ballads of a really fine and spirited character.
10056{_}1938{_}Sometimes the poems celebrate the common pursuits, occupations, and incidents of life.
10056{_}1939{_}They rise to the exaltation of the epithalamium, or of the vintage song; at other times they deal with sentiment and human conduct, being in the highest degree sententious and epigrammatic.
10056{_}1940{_}We must give the credit to Confucius of having saved for us the literature of China, and of having set his people an example in preserving the monuments of a remote antiquity.
10056{_}1941{_}While the literatures of ancient Greece and Rome have largely perished in the convulsions that followed the breaking up of the Roman empire in Europe, when the kingdom of China fell into disorder and decrepitude this one great teacher stepped forward to save the precious record of historic fact, philosophical thought, and of legislation as well as poetry, from being swept away by the deluge of revolution.
10056{_}1942{_}Confucius showed his wisdom by the high value he set upon the poetry of his native land, and his name must be set side by side with that of the astute tyrant of Athens who collected the poems of Homer and preserved them as a precious heritage to the Greek world.
10056{_}1943{_}Confucius has given us his opinion with regard to the poems of the Shi-King.
10056{_}1944{_}No man, he says, is worth speaking to who has not mastered the poems of an anthology, the perusal of which elevates the mind and purifies it from all corrupt thoughts.
10056{_}1945{_}Thanks to the work of modern scholarship, English readers can now verify this dictum for themselves.
10056{_}1946{_}E. W.     THE SHI-KING    _PART I--LESSONS FROM THE STATES_   BOOK I  THE ODES OF CHOW AND THE SOUTH   ~Celebrating the Virtue of King Wan's Bride~     Hark!
10056{_}1947{_}from the islet in the stream the voice   Of the fish-hawks that o'er their nests rejoice!
10056{_}1948{_}From them our thoughts to that young lady go,   Modest and virtuous, loth herself to show.
10056{_}1949{_}Where could be found to share our prince's state,   So fair, so virtuous, and so fit a mate?
10056{_}1950{_}See how the duckweed's stalks, or short or long,   Sway left and right, as moves the current strong!
10056{_}1951{_}So hard it was for him the maid to find!
10056{_}1952{_}By day, by night, our prince with constant mind   Sought for her long, but all his search was vain.
10056{_}1953{_}Awake, asleep, he ever felt the pain   Of longing thought, as when on restless bed,   Tossing about, one turns his fevered head.
10056{_}1954{_}Here long, there short, afloat the duckweed lies;   But caught at last, we seize the longed-for prize.
10056{_}1955{_}The maiden modest, virtuous, coy, is found;   Strike every lute, and joyous welcome sound.
10056{_}1956{_}Ours now, the duckweed from the stream we bear,   And cook to use with other viands rare.
10056{_}1957{_}He has the maiden, modest, virtuous, bright;   Let bells and drums proclaim our great delight    ~Celebrating the Industry of King Wan's Queen~    Sweet was the scene.
10056{_}1958{_}The spreading dolichos   Extended far, down to the valley's depths,   With leaves luxuriant.
10056{_}1959{_}The orioles   Fluttered around, and on the bushy trees   In throngs collected--whence their pleasant notes   Resounded far in richest melody.
10056{_}1960{_}The spreading dolichos extended far,   Covering the valley's sides, down to its depths,   With leaves luxuriant and dense.
10056{_}1961{_}I cut   It down, then boiled, and from the fibres spun   Of cloth, both fine and coarse, large store,   To wear, unwearied of such simple dress.
10056{_}1962{_}Now back to my old home, my parents dear   To see, I go.
10056{_}1963{_}The matron I have told,   Who will announcement make.
10056{_}1964{_}Meanwhile my clothes,   My private clothes I wash, and rinse my robes.
10056{_}1965{_}Which of them need be rinsed?
10056{_}1966{_}and which need not?
10056{_}1967{_}My parents dear to visit, back I go.
10056{_}1968{_}~In Praise of a Bride~    Graceful and young the peach-tree stands;     How rich its flowers, all gleaming bright!
10056{_}1969{_}This bride to her new home repairs;     Chamber and house she'll order right.
10056{_}1970{_}Graceful and young the peach-tree stands;     Large crops of fruit it soon will show.
10056{_}1971{_}This bride to her new home repairs;     Chamber and house her sway shall know.

10056{_}1986{_}~Lamenting the Absence of a Cherished Friend~    Though small my basket, all my toil     Filled it with mouse-ears but in part.

10056{_}1993{_}~Celebrating the Goodness of the Descendants of King Wan~    As the feet of the _lin_, which avoid each living thing,   So our prince's noble sons no harm to men will bring.
10056{_}1994{_}They are the _lin!_    As the front of the _lin_, never forward thrust in wrath,   So our prince's noble grandsons of love tread the path.
10056{_}1995{_}They are the _lin!_    As the horn of the _lin_, flesh-tipped, no wound to give,   So our prince's noble kindred kindly with all live.
10056{_}1996{_}They are the _lin!_  [NOTE.--The "lin" is the female of "K'e"--a fabulous animal--the symbol of all goodness and benevolence; having the body of a deer, the tail of an ox, the hoofs of a horse, one horn, the scales of a fish, etc.
10056{_}1997{_}Its feet do not tread on any living thing--not even on live grass; it does not butt with its forehead; and the end of its horn is covered with flesh--to show that, while able for war, it wills to have peace.
10056{_}1998{_}The "lin" was supposed to appear inaugurating a golden age, but the poet finds a better auspice of that in the character of Wan's family and kindred.]

10056{_}2080{_}BOOK III    THE ODES OF P'EI   ~An Officer Bewails the Neglect with which He is Treated~    It floats about, that boat of cypress wood,     Now here, now there, as by the current borne.
10056{_}2081{_}Nor rest nor sleep comes in my troubled mood;     I suffer as when painful wound has torn     The shrinking body.
10056{_}2082{_}Thus I dwell forlorn,   And aimless muse, my thoughts of sorrow full.
10056{_}2083{_}I might with wine refresh my spirit worn;   I might go forth, and, sauntering try to cool   The fever of my heart; but grief holds sullen rule.
10056{_}2084{_}My mind resembles not a mirror plate,     Reflecting all the impressions it receives.
10056{_}2085{_}The good I love, the bad regard with hate;     I only cherish whom my heart believes.
10056{_}2086{_}Colleagues I have, but yet my spirit grieves,   That on their honor I cannot depend.
10056{_}2087{_}I speak, but my complaint no influence leaves   Upon their hearts; with mine no feelings blend;   With me in anger they, and fierce disdain contend.
10056{_}2088{_}My mind is fixed, and cannot, like a stone,     Be turned at will indifferently about;   And what I think, to that, and that alone,     I utterance give, alike within, without;     Nor can like mat be rolled and carried out.
10056{_}2089{_}With dignity in presence of them all,     My conduct marked, my goodness who shall scout?
10056{_}2090{_}My foes I boldly challenge, great and small,   If there be aught in me they can in question call.
10056{_}2091{_}How full of trouble is my anxious heart!
10056{_}2092{_}With hate the blatant herd of creatures mean   Ceaseless pursue.
10056{_}2093{_}Of their attacks the smart     Keeps my mind in distress.
10056{_}2094{_}Their venomed spleen     Aye vents itself; and with insulting mien   They vex my soul; and no one on my side     A word will speak.
10056{_}2095{_}Silent, alone, unseen,   I think of my sad case; then opening wide   My eyes, as if from sleep, I beat my breast, sore-tried.
10056{_}2096{_}Thy disc, O sun, should ever be complete,     While thine, O changing moon, doth wax and wane.
10056{_}2097{_}But now our sun hath waned, weak and effete,     And moons are ever full.
10056{_}2098{_}My heart with pain     Is firmly bound, and held in sorrow's chain,   As to the body cleaves an unwashed dress.
10056{_}2099{_}Silent I think of my sad case; in vain   I try to find relief from my distress.
10056{_}2100{_}Would I had wings to fly where ills no longer press!

10056{_}2111{_}~The Plaint of a Rejected Wife~    The east wind gently blows,     With cloudy skies and rain.
10056{_}2112{_}'Twixt man and wife should ne'er be strife,     But harmony obtain.

10056{_}2114{_}I go along the road,     Slow, with reluctant heart.
10056{_}2115{_}Your escort lame to door but came,     There glad from me to part.

10056{_}2126{_}The stores for winter piled    Are all unprized in spring.

10056{_}2218{_}Those robes your character beseem;     When they are worn we'll make you new.
10056{_}2219{_}Now for your court!
10056{_}2220{_}oh!
10056{_}2221{_}there we deem     It pleasure great your form to view.
10056{_}2222{_}And when we to our homes repair,       We'll send to you our richest fare,       Such is the love to you we bear!
10056{_}2223{_}~A Wife Consoled by Her Husband's Arrival~    Cold is the wind, fast falls the rain,     The cock aye shrilly crows.
10056{_}2224{_}But I have seen my lord again;--     Now must my heart repose.
10056{_}2225{_}Whistles the wind, patters the rain,     The cock's crow far resounds.
10056{_}2226{_}But I have seen my lord again,     And healed are my heart's wounds.
10056{_}2227{_}All's dark amid the wind and rain,     Ceaseless the cock's clear voice!
10056{_}2228{_}But I have seen my lord again;--     Should not my heart rejoice?
10056{_}2229{_}~In Praise of Some Lady~    There by his side in chariot rideth she,   As lovely flower of the hibiscus tree,   So fair her face; and when about they wheel,   Her girdle gems of _Ken_ themselves reveal.
10056{_}2230{_}For beauty all the House of Keang have fame;   Its eldest daughter--she beseems her name.
10056{_}2231{_}There on the path, close by him, walketh she,   Bright as the blossom of hibiscus tree,   And fair her face; and when around they flit,   Her girdle gems a tinkling sound emit.
10056{_}2232{_}Among the Keang she has distinguished place,   For virtuous fame renowned, and peerless grace.
10056{_}2233{_}~A Man's Praise of His Wife~    My path forth from the east gate lay,   Where cloud-like moved the girls at play.
10056{_}2234{_}Numerous are they, as clouds so bright,   But not on them my heart's thoughts light.
10056{_}2235{_}Dressed in a thin white silk, with coiffure gray   Is she, my wife, my joy in life's low way.
10056{_}2236{_}Forth by the covering wall's high tower,   I went, and saw, like rush in flower,   Each flaunting girl.
10056{_}2237{_}Brilliant are they,   But not with them my heart's thoughts stay.
10056{_}2238{_}In thin white silk, with head-dress madder-dyed,   Is she, my sole delight, 'foretime my bride.
10056{_}2239{_}~An Entreaty~    Along the great highway,     I hold you by the cuff.
10056{_}2240{_}O spurn me not, I pray,     Nor break old friendship off.
10056{_}2241{_}Along the highway worn,     I hold your hand in mine.
10056{_}2242{_}Do not as vile me scorn;     Your love I can't resign.

10056{_}2244{_}that artful boy     Refuses me a word!

10056{_}2246{_}O dear!
10056{_}2247{_}that artful boy     My table will not share!
10056{_}2248{_}But, Sir, I shall enjoy     My rest, though you're not there!
10056{_}2249{_}~A Lady Mourns the Absence of Her Student Lover~    You student, with the collar blue,     Long pines my heart with anxious pain.
10056{_}2250{_}Although I do not go to you,     Why from all word do you refrain?
10056{_}2251{_}O you, with girdle strings of blue,     My thoughts to you forever roam!
10056{_}2252{_}Although I do not go to you,     Yet why to me should you not come?
10056{_}2253{_}How reckless you, how light and wild,     There by the tower upon the wall!
10056{_}2254{_}One day, from sight of you exiled,     As long as three long months I call.

10056{_}2256{_}BOOK VIII    THE ODES OF TS'E    ~A Wife Urging Her Husband to Action~    His lady to the marquis says,      "The cock has crowed; 'tis late.
10056{_}2257{_}Get up, my lord, and haste to court.
10056{_}2258{_}'Tis full; for you they wait."
10056{_}2259{_}She did not hear the cock's shrill sound,   Only the blueflies buzzing round.
10056{_}2260{_}Again she wakes him with the words,      "The east, my lord, is bright.
10056{_}2261{_}A crowded court your presence seeks;      Get up and hail the light."
10056{_}2262{_}'Twas not the dawning light which shone,   But that which by the moon was thrown.

10056{_}2265{_}~The Folly of Useless Effort~    The weeds will but the ranker grow,     If fields too large you seek to till.
10056{_}2266{_}To try to gain men far away     With grief your toiling heart will fill,    If fields too large you seek to till,     The weeds will only rise more strong.
10056{_}2267{_}To try to gain men far away     Will but your heart's distress prolong.
10056{_}2268{_}Things grow the best when to themselves     Left, and to nature's vigor rare.
10056{_}2269{_}How young and tender is the child,     With his twin tufts of falling hair!
10056{_}2270{_}But when you him ere long behold,     That child shall cap of manhood wear!
10056{_}2271{_}~The Prince of Loo~    A grand man is the prince of Loo,     With person large and high.
10056{_}2272{_}Lofty his front and suited to     The fine glance of his eye!
10056{_}2273{_}Swift are his feet.
10056{_}2274{_}In archery     What man with him can vie?
10056{_}2275{_}With all these goodly qualities,     We see him and we sigh!
10056{_}2276{_}Renowned through all the land is he,     The nephew of our lord.
10056{_}2277{_}With clear and lovely eyes, his grace     May not be told by word.
10056{_}2278{_}All day at target practice,     He'll never miss the bird.
10056{_}2279{_}Such is the prince of Loo, and yet     With grief for him we're stirred!
10056{_}2280{_}All grace and beauty he displays,     High forehead and eyes bright.
10056{_}2281{_}And dancing choice!
10056{_}2282{_}His arrows all     The target hit aright.
10056{_}2283{_}Straight through they go, and every one     Lights on the self-same spot.
10056{_}2284{_}Rebellion he could well withstand,     And yet we mourn his lot!
10056{_}2285{_}BOOK IX    THE ODES OF WEI    ~On the Misgovernment of the State~    A fruit, small as the garden peach,     May still be used for food.
10056{_}2286{_}A State, though poor as ours, might thrive,     If but its rule were good.
10056{_}2287{_}Our rule is bad, our State is sad,     With mournful heart I grieve.
10056{_}2288{_}All can from instrument and voice     My mood of mind perceive.
10056{_}2289{_}Who know me not, with scornful thought,     Deem me a scholar proud.
10056{_}2290{_}"Those men are right," they fiercely say,     "What mean your words so loud?"
10056{_}2291{_}Deep in my heart my sorrows lie,     And none the cause may know.
10056{_}2292{_}How should they know who never try     To learn whence comes our woe?
10056{_}2293{_}The garden jujube, although small,     May still be used for food.
10056{_}2294{_}A State, though poor as ours, might thrive,     If but its rule were good.
10056{_}2295{_}Our rule is bad, our State is sad,     With mournful heart I grieve.
10056{_}2296{_}Methinks I'll wander through the land,     My misery to relieve.
10056{_}2297{_}Who know me not, with scornful thought,     Deem that wild views I hold.
10056{_}2298{_}"Those men are right," they fiercely say,     "What mean your words so bold?"
10056{_}2299{_}Deep in my heart my sorrows lie,   And none the cause may know.
10056{_}2300{_}How can they know, who never try   To learn whence comes our woe?
10056{_}2301{_}~The Mean Husband~    Thin cloth of dolichos supplies the shoes,     In which some have to brave the frost and cold.
10056{_}2302{_}A bride, when poor, her tender hands must use,     Her dress to make, and the sharp needle hold.
10056{_}2303{_}This man is wealthy, yet he makes his bride     Collars and waistbands for his robes provide.
10056{_}2304{_}Conscious of wealth, he moves with easy mien;     Politely on the left he takes his place;   The ivory pin is at his girdle seen:--     His dress and gait show gentlemanly grace.
10056{_}2305{_}Why do we brand him in our satire here?
10056{_}2306{_}'Tis this---his niggard soul provokes the sneer.
10056{_}2307{_}~A Young Soldier on Service~    To the top of that tree-clad hill I go,     And towards my father I gaze,   Till with my mind's eye his form I espy,     And my mind's ear hears how he says:--   "Alas for my son on service abroad!
10056{_}2308{_}He rests not from morning till eve.
10056{_}2309{_}May he careful be and come back to me!
10056{_}2310{_}While he is away, how I grieve!"
10056{_}2311{_}To the top of that barren hill I climb,     And towards my mother I gaze,   Till with my mind's eye her form I espy,     And my mind's ear hears how she says:--   "Alas for my child on service abroad!
10056{_}2312{_}He never in sleep shuts an eye.
10056{_}2313{_}May he careful be, and come back to me!
10056{_}2314{_}In the wild may his body not lie!"

10056{_}2316{_}my young brother, serving abroad,     All day with his comrades must roam.
10056{_}2317{_}May he careful be, and come back to me,     And die not away from his home."
10056{_}2318{_}BOOK X    THE ODES OF TANG    ~The King Goes to War~    The wild geese fly the bushy oaks around,   With clamor loud.

10056{_}2321{_}Our millet still unsown, we haste away.

10056{_}2352{_}~A Wife Mourns for Her Husband~    The dolichos grows and covers the thorn,     O'er the waste is the dragon-plant creeping.
10056{_}2353{_}The man of my heart is away and I mourn--     What home have I, lonely and weeping?
10056{_}2354{_}Covering the jujubes the dolichos grows,     The graves many dragon-plants cover;   But where is the man on whose breast I'd repose?
10056{_}2355{_}No home have I, having no lover!

10056{_}2357{_}While the long days of summer pass over my head,     And long winter nights leave their traces,   I'm alone!

10056{_}2359{_}Through the long winter nights I am burdened with fears,     Through the long summer days I am lonely;   But when time shall have counted its hundreds of years     I then shall be his--and his only!
10056{_}2360{_}BOOK XI    THE ODES OF TS'IN    ~Celebrating the Opulence of the Lords of Ts'in~    Our ruler to the hunt proceeds;   And black as iron are his steeds   That heed the charioteer's command,   Who holds the six reins in his hand.

10056{_}2401{_}[NOTE.--The incident related in this poem occurred in the year B.C.
10056{_}2402{_}620, when the duke of Muh died after playing an important part in the affairs of Northwest China.
10056{_}2403{_}Muh required the three officers here celebrated, to be buried with him, and according to the "Historical Records" this barbarous practice began with duke Ching, Muh's predecessor.
10056{_}2404{_}In all, 170 individuals were buried with Muh.
10056{_}2405{_}The death of the last distinguished man of the Ts'in dynasty, the Emperor I, was subsequently celebrated by the entombment with him of all the inmates of his harem.]
10056{_}2406{_}~In Praise of a Ruler of Ts'in~    What trees grow on the Chung-nan hill?

10056{_}2409{_}His face glows with vermilion hue.
10056{_}2410{_}O may he prove a ruler true!

10056{_}2413{_}Our prince shows there the double _Ke_     On lower robe displayed.
10056{_}2414{_}His pendant holds each tinkling gem,   Long life be his, and deathless fame!

10056{_}2419{_}BOOK XII    THE ODES OF CH'IN    ~The Contentment of a Poor Recluse~  My only door some pieces of crossed wood,   Within it I can rest enjoy.

10056{_}2421{_}Purged from ambition's aims I say, "For fish.
10056{_}2422{_}We need not bream caught in the Ho; Nor, to possess the sweets of love, require   To Ts'e, to find a Keang, to go.
10056{_}2423{_}"The man contented with his lot, a meal   Of fish without Ho carp can make; Nor needs, to rest in his domestic joy,   A Tsze of Sung as wife to take."

10056{_}2434{_}~The Lament of a Lover~  There where its shores the marsh surround, Rushes and lotus plants abound.

10056{_}2439{_}But lovelier is that Beauty rare, Handsome and large, and tall and fair, I wish and long to call her mine, Doomed with the longing still to pine.
10056{_}2440{_}Nor day nor night e'er brings relief; My inmost heart is full of grief.
10056{_}2441{_}Around the marsh, in rich display, Grow rush and lotus flowers, all gay.
10056{_}2442{_}But not with her do they compare, So tall and large, majestic, fair.
10056{_}2443{_}Both day and night, I nothing speed; Still clings to me the aching need.
10056{_}2444{_}On side, on back, on face, I lie, But vain each change of posture.
