10056{_}2452{_}BOOK XIV    THE ODES OF TS'AOU  ~Against Frivolous Pursuits~    Like splendid robes appear the wings     Of the ephemeral fly;   And such the pomp of those great men,     Which soon in death shall lie!
10056{_}2453{_}I grieve!
10056{_}2454{_}Would they but come to me!
10056{_}2455{_}To teach them I should try.
10056{_}2456{_}The wings of the ephemeral fly     Are robes of colors gay;   And such the glory of those men,     Soon crumbling to decay!
10056{_}2457{_}I grieve!
10056{_}2458{_}Would they but rest with me,     They'd learn a better way!
10056{_}2459{_}The ephemeral fly bursts from its hole,     With gauzy wings like snow;   So quick the rise, so quick the fall,     Of those great men we know!
10056{_}2460{_}I grieve!
10056{_}2461{_}Would they but lodge with me,     Forth they would wiser go.
10056{_}2462{_}BOOK XV    THE ODES OF PIN   ~The Duke of Chow Tells of His Soldiers~    To the hills of the east we went,     And long had we there to remain.
10056{_}2463{_}When the word of recall was sent,     Thick and fast came the drizzling rain.
10056{_}2464{_}When told our return we should take,     Our hearts in the West were and sore;   But there did they clothes for us make:--     They knew our hard service was o'er.
10056{_}2465{_}On the mulberry grounds in our sight     The large caterpillars were creeping;   Lonely and still we passed the night,     All under our carriages sleeping.
10056{_}2466{_}To the hills of the East we went,     And long had we there to remain.
10056{_}2467{_}When the word of recall was sent,     Thick and fast came the drizzling rain.
10056{_}2468{_}The heavenly gourds rise to the eye,     With their fruit hanging under the eave.
10056{_}2469{_}In our chambers the sow-bug we spy;     Their webs on our doors spiders weave.
10056{_}2470{_}Our paddocks seem crowded with deer,     With the glow-worm's light all about.
10056{_}2471{_}Such thoughts, while they filled us with fear,     We tried, but in vain, to keep out.
10056{_}2472{_}To the hills of the East we went,     And long had we there to remain.
10056{_}2473{_}When the word of recall was sent,     Thick and fast came the drizzling rain.
10056{_}2474{_}On ant-hills screamed cranes with delight;     In their rooms were our wives sighing sore.
10056{_}2475{_}Our homes they had swept and made tight:--     All at once we arrived at the door.
10056{_}2476{_}The bitter gourds hanging are seen,     From branches of chestnut-trees high.
10056{_}2477{_}Three years of toil away we had been,     Since such a sight greeted the eye.
10056{_}2478{_}To the hills of the East we went,     And long had we there to remain.
10056{_}2479{_}When the word of recall was sent,     Thick and fast came the drizzling rain.
10056{_}2480{_}With its wings now here, and now there,     Is the oriole sporting in flight.
10056{_}2481{_}Those brides to their husbands repair,     Their steeds red and bay, flecked with white.
10056{_}2482{_}Each mother has fitted each sash;     Their equipments are full and complete;   But fresh unions, whatever their dash,     Can ne'er with reunions compete.
10056{_}2483{_}~There is a Proper Way for Doing Everything~    In hewing an axe-shaft, how must you act?
10056{_}2484{_}Another axe take, or you'll never succeed.
10056{_}2485{_}In taking a wife, be sure 'tis a fact,     That with no go-between you never can speed.
10056{_}2486{_}In hewing an axe-shaft, hewing a shaft,     For a copy you have the axe in your hand.
10056{_}2487{_}In choosing a wife, you follow the craft,     And forthwith on the mats the feast-vessels stand.
10056{_}2488{_}PART II.--MINOR ODES TO THE KINGDOM   BOOK I    DECADE OF LUH MING   ~A Festal Ode~    With sounds of happiness the deer     Browse on the celery of the meads.
10056{_}2489{_}A nobler feast is furnished here,     With guests renowned for noble deeds.
10056{_}2490{_}The lutes are struck; the organ blows,     Till all its tongues in movement heave.
10056{_}2491{_}Each basket loaded stands, and shows     The precious gifts the guests receive.
10056{_}2492{_}They love me and my mind will teach,   How duty's highest aim to reach.
10056{_}2493{_}With sounds of happiness the deer     The southern-wood crop in the meads,   What noble guests surround me here,     Distinguished for their worthy deeds!
10056{_}2494{_}From them my people learn to fly     Whate'er is mean; to chiefs they give   A model and a pattern high;--     They show the life they ought to live.
10056{_}2495{_}Then fill their cups with spirits rare,   Till each the banquet's joy shall share.
10056{_}2496{_}With sounds of happiness the deer     The salsola crop in the fields.
10056{_}2497{_}What noble guests surround me here!
10056{_}2498{_}Each lute for them its music yields.
10056{_}2499{_}Sound, sound the lutes, or great or small.
10056{_}2500{_}The joy harmonious to prolong;--    And with my spirits rich crown all     The cups to cheer the festive throng.
10056{_}2501{_}Let each retire with gladdened heart,   In his own sphere to play his part.
10056{_}2502{_}~A Festal Ode Complimenting an Officer~    On dashed my four steeds, without halt, without stay,   Though toilsome and winding from Chow was the way.
10056{_}2503{_}I wished to return--but the monarch's command   Forbade that his business be done with slack hand;     And my heart was with sadness oppressed.
10056{_}2504{_}On dashed my four steeds; I ne'er slackened the reins.
10056{_}2505{_}They snorted and panted--all white, with black manes.
10056{_}2506{_}I wished to return, but our sovereign's command   Forbade that his business be done with slack hand;--     And I dared not to pause or to rest.
10056{_}2507{_}Unresting the Filial doves speed in their flight,   Ascending, then sweeping swift down from the height,   Now grouped on the oaks.
10056{_}2508{_}The king's high command   Forbade that his business be done with slack hand;--     And my father I left, sore distressed.
10056{_}2509{_}Unresting the Filial doves speed in their flight,   Now fanning the air and anon they alight   On the medlars thick grouped.
10056{_}2510{_}But our monarch's command   Forbade that his business be done with slack hand;--     Of my mother I thought with sad breast.
10056{_}2511{_}My four steeds I harnessed, all white and black-maned,   Which straight on their way, fleet and emulous strained.
10056{_}2512{_}I wished to return; and now venture in song   The wish to express, and announce how I long     For my mother my care to attest.
10056{_}2513{_}[NOTE.--Both Maou and Choo agree that this ode was composed in honor of the officer who narrates the story in it, although they say it was not written by the officer himself, but was put into his mouth, as it were, to express the sympathy of his entertainer with him, and the appreciation of his devotion to duty.]
10056{_}2514{_}~The Value of Friendship~    The woodmen's blows responsive ring,     As on the trees they fall;   And when the birds their sweet notes sing,     They to each other call.
10056{_}2515{_}From the dark valley comes a bird,     And seeks the lofty tree.
10056{_}2516{_}_Ying_ goes its voice, and thus it cries,     "Companion, come to me."
10056{_}2517{_}The bird, although a creature small,     Upon its mate depends;   And shall we men, who rank o'er all,     Not seek to have our friends?
10056{_}2518{_}All spirits love the friendly man,     And hearken to his prayer.
10056{_}2519{_}What harmony and peace they can     Bestow, his lot shall share.
10056{_}2520{_}_Hoo-hoo_ the woodmen all unite     To shout, as trees they fell.
10056{_}2521{_}They do their work with all their might;--     What I have done I'll tell.
10056{_}2522{_}I've strained and made my spirits clear,     The fatted lambs I've killed.
10056{_}2523{_}With friends who my own surname bear,     My hall I've largely filled.
10056{_}2524{_}Some may be absent, casually,     And leave a broken line;   But better this than absence by     An oversight of mine.
10056{_}2525{_}My court I've sprinkled and swept clean,     Viands in order set.
10056{_}2526{_}Eight dishes loaded stand with grain;     There's store of fatted meat.
10056{_}2527{_}My mother's kith and kin I'm sure     I've widely called by name.
10056{_}2528{_}That some be hindered better is     Than ~I~ give cause for blame.
10056{_}2529{_}On the hill-side the trees they fell,     All working with good-will   I labor too, with equal zeal.
10056{_}2530{_}And the host's part fulfil.
10056{_}2531{_}Spirits I've set in order meet,     The dishes stand in rows.
10056{_}2532{_}The guests are here; no vacant seat     A brother absent shows.
10056{_}2533{_}The loss of kindly feeling oft     From slightest things shall grow,   Where all the fare is dry and spare,     Resentments fierce may glow.
10056{_}2534{_}My store of spirits is well strained,     If short prove the supply,   My messengers I straightway send,     And what is needed buy.
10056{_}2535{_}I beat the drums, and in the dance     Lead joyously the train.
10056{_}2536{_}Oh!
10056{_}2537{_}good it is, when falls the chance     The sparkling cup to drain.
10056{_}2538{_}~The Response to a Festal Ode~    Heaven shields and sets thee fast.
10056{_}2539{_}It round thee fair has cast     Thy virtue pure.
10056{_}2540{_}Thus richest joy is thine;--   Increase of corn and wine,   And every gift divine,     Abundant, sure.
10056{_}2541{_}Heaven shields and sets thee fast.
10056{_}2542{_}From it thou goodness hast;     Right are thy ways.
10056{_}2543{_}Its choicest gifts 'twill pour,   That last for evermore,   Nor time exhaust the store     Through endless days.
10056{_}2544{_}Heaven shields and sets thee fast,   Makes thine endeavor last     And prosper well.
10056{_}2545{_}Like hills and mountains high,   Whose masses touch the sky;   Like streams aye surging by;     Thine increase swell!
10056{_}2546{_}With rite and auspice fair,   Thine offerings thou dost bear,     And son-like give,   The season's round from spring,   To olden duke and king,   Whose words to thee we bring:--     "Forever live,"    The spirits of thy dead   Pour blessings on thy head,     Unnumbered sweet.
10056{_}2547{_}Thy subjects, simple, good,   Enjoy their drink and food.
10056{_}2548{_}Our tribes of every blood     Follow thy feet.
10056{_}2549{_}Like moons that wax in light;   Or suns that scale the height;     Or ageless hill;   Nor change, nor autumn know;   As pine and cypress grow;   The sons that from thee flow     Be lasting still!
10056{_}2550{_}~An Ode of Congratulation~    The russet pear-tree stands there all alone;   How bright the growth of fruit upon it shown!
10056{_}2551{_}The King's affairs no stinting hands require,   And days prolonged still mock our fond desire.
10056{_}2552{_}But time has brought the tenth month of the year;   My woman's heart is torn with wound severe.
10056{_}2553{_}Surely my warrior lord might now appear!
10056{_}2554{_}The russet pear-tree stands there all alone;   How dense the leafy shade all o'er it thrown!
10056{_}2555{_}The King's affairs require no slackening hand,   And our sad hearts their feelings can't command.
10056{_}2556{_}The plants and trees in beauty shine; 'tis spring.
10056{_}2557{_}From off my heart its gloom I fain would fling.
10056{_}2558{_}This season well my warrior home may bring!
10056{_}2559{_}I climbed that northern hill, and medlars sought;   The spring nigh o'er, to ripeness they were brought.
10056{_}2560{_}"The King's affairs cannot be slackly done";--   'Tis thus our parents mourn their absent son.
10056{_}2561{_}But now his sandal car must broken be;   I seem his powerful steeds worn out to see.
10056{_}2562{_}Relief has gone!
10056{_}2563{_}He can't be far from me!
10056{_}2564{_}Alas!
10056{_}2565{_}they can't have marched; they don't arrive!
10056{_}2566{_}More hard it grows with my distress to strive.
10056{_}2567{_}The time is passed, and still he is not here!
10056{_}2568{_}My sorrows multiply; great is my fear.
10056{_}2569{_}But lo!
10056{_}2570{_}by reeds and shell I have divined,   That he is near, they both assure my mind;--   Soon at my side my warrior I shall find!
10056{_}2571{_}~An Ode on the Return of the Troops~    Forth from the city in our cars we drove,     Until we halted at the pasture ground.
10056{_}2572{_}The general came, and there with ardor strove     A note of zeal throughout the host to sound.
10056{_}2573{_}"Direct from court I come, by orders bound   The march to hasten";--it was thus he spake.
10056{_}2574{_}Then with the carriage-officers around,   He strictly charged them quick despatch to make:--   "Urgent the King's affairs, forthwith the field we take."
10056{_}2575{_}While there we stopped, the second corps appeared,     And 'twixt Us and the city took its place.
10056{_}2576{_}The guiding standard was on high upreared,     Where twining snakes the tortoises embrace,     While oxtails, crest-like, did the staff's top grace.
10056{_}2577{_}We watched the sheet unfolding grandly wave;     Each flag around showed falcons on its face.
10056{_}2578{_}With anxious care looked on our leader brave;   Watchful the carriage-officers appeared and grave.
10056{_}2579{_}Nan Chung, our chief, had heard the royal call     To go where inroad by Heen-yuns was made,   And 'cross the frontier build a barrier wall.
10056{_}2580{_}Numerous his chariots, splendidly arrayed!
10056{_}2581{_}The standards--this where dragons were displayed,   And that where snakes round tortoises were coiled--     Terrific flew.
10056{_}2582{_}"Northward our host," he said,   "Heaven's son sends forth to tame the Heen-yun wild."
10056{_}2583{_}Soon by this awful chief would all their tribes be foiled.
10056{_}2584{_}When first we took the field, and northward went,     The millet was in flower;--a prospect sweet.
10056{_}2585{_}Now when our weary steps are homeward bent,     The snow falls fast, the mire impedes our feet.
10056{_}2586{_}Many the hardships we were called to meet,   Ere the King's orders we had all fulfilled.
10056{_}2587{_}No rest we had; often our friends to greet   The longing came; but vain regrets we stilled;   By tablets stern our hearts with fresh resolve were thrilled.
10056{_}2588{_}"Incessant chirp the insects in the grass;     All round about the nimble hoppers spring.
10056{_}2589{_}From them our thoughts quick to our husbands pass?
10056{_}2590{_}Although those thoughts our hearts with anguish wring.
10056{_}2591{_}Oh!
10056{_}2592{_}could we see them, what relief 'twould bring!
10056{_}2593{_}Our hearts, rejoiced, at once would feel at rest."
10056{_}2594{_}Thus did our wives, their case deploring, sing;   The while our leader farther on had pressed,   And smitten with his power the wild Jung of the west.
10056{_}2595{_}The spring days now are lengthening out their light;     The plants and trees are dressed in living green;   The orioles resting sing, or wing their flight;     Our wives amid the southern-wood are seen,     Which white they bring, to feed their silkworms keen.
10056{_}2596{_}Our host, returned, sweeps onwards to the hall,     Where chiefs are questioned, shown the captives mean   Nan Chung, majestic, draws the gaze of all,   Proud o'er the barbarous foe his victories to recall.
10056{_}2597{_}BOOK II    THE DECADE OF PIH H'WA    ~An Ode Appropriate to a Festivity~    The dew lies heavy all around,   Nor, till the sun shines, leaves the ground.
10056{_}2598{_}Far into night we feasting sit;   We drink, and none his place may quit.
10056{_}2599{_}The dew lies heavy, and its gems   Stud the luxuriant, grassy stems.
10056{_}2600{_}The happy night with wassail rings;   So feasted here the former kings.
10056{_}2601{_}The jujube and the willow-tree   All fretted with the dew we see.
10056{_}2602{_}Each guest's a prince of noble line,   In whom the virtues all combine.
10056{_}2603{_}The _t'ung_ and _e_ their fruits display,   Pendant from every graceful spray.
10056{_}2604{_}My guests are joyous and serene,   No haggard eye, no ruffled mien.
10056{_}2605{_}BOOK III    THE DECADE OF TUNG RUNG    ~Celebrating a Hunting Expedition~    Our chariots were well-built and firm,     Well-matched our steeds, and fleet and strong.
10056{_}2606{_}Four, sleek and large, each chariot drew,     And eastward thus we drove along.
10056{_}2607{_}Our hunting cars were light and good,     Each with its team of noble steeds.
10056{_}2608{_}Still further east we took the way     To Foo-mere's grassy plains that leads.
10056{_}2609{_}Loud-voiced, the masters of the chase     Arranged the huntsmen, high and low.
10056{_}2610{_}While banners streamed, and ox-tails flew,     We sought the prey on distant Gaou.
10056{_}2611{_}Each with full team, the princes came,     A lengthened train in bright array.
10056{_}2612{_}In gold-wrought slippers, knee-caps red,     They looked as on an audience day.
10056{_}2613{_}Each right thumb wore the metal guard;     On the left arm its shield was bound.
10056{_}2614{_}In unison the arrows flew;     The game lay piled upon the ground.
10056{_}2615{_}The leaders of the tawny teams     Sped on their course, direct and true.
10056{_}2616{_}The drivers perfect skill displayed;     Like blow well aimed each arrow flew.
10056{_}2617{_}Neighing and pleased, the steeds returned;   The bannered lines back slowly came.
10056{_}2618{_}No jostling rude disgraced the crowd;   The king declined large share of game.
10056{_}2619{_}So did this famous hunt proceed!
10056{_}2620{_}So free it was from clamorous sound!
10056{_}2621{_}Well does our King become his place, And high the deeds his reign have crowned!
10056{_}2622{_}~The King's Anxiety for His Morning Levee~    How goes the night?
10056{_}2623{_}For heavy morning sleep   Ill suits the king who men would loyal keep.
10056{_}2624{_}The courtyard, ruddy with the torch's light,   Proclaims unspent the deepest hour of night.
10056{_}2625{_}Already near the gate my lords appear;   Their tinkling bells salute my wakeful ear.
10056{_}2626{_}How goes the night?
10056{_}2627{_}I may not slumber on.
10056{_}2628{_}Although not yet the night is wholly gone,   The paling torch-light in the court below   Gives token that the hours swift-footed go.
10056{_}2629{_}Already at the gate my lords appear;   Their tinkling bells with measured sound draw near.
10056{_}2630{_}How goes the night?
10056{_}2631{_}I may not slumber now.
10056{_}2632{_}The darkness smiles with morning on its brow.
10056{_}2633{_}The courtyard torch no more gives forth its ray,   But heralds with its smoke the coming day.
10056{_}2634{_}My princes pass the gate, and gather there;   I see their banners floating in the air.
10056{_}2635{_}~Moral Lessons from Natural Facts~    All true words fly, as from yon reedy marsh   The crane rings o'er the wild its screaming harsh.
10056{_}2636{_}Vainly you try reason in chains to keep;--   Freely it moves as fish sweeps through the deep.
10056{_}2637{_}Hate follows love, as 'neath those sandal-trees   The withered leaves the eager searcher sees.
10056{_}2638{_}The hurtful ne'er without some good was born;--   The stones that mar the hill will grind the corn.
10056{_}2639{_}All true words spread, as from the marsh's eye   The crane's sonorous note ascends the sky.
10056{_}2640{_}Goodness throughout the widest sphere abides,   As fish round isle and through the ocean glides.
10056{_}2641{_}And lesser good near greater you shall see,   As grows the paper shrub 'neath sandal-tree.
10056{_}2642{_}And good emerges from what man condemns;--   Those stones that mar the hill will polish gems.
10056{_}2643{_}BOOK IV    THE DECADE OF K'E-FOO    ~On the Completion of a Royal Palace~    On yonder banks a palace, lo!
10056{_}2644{_}upshoots,     The tender blue of southern hill behind;   Firm-founded, like the bamboo's clamping roots;     Its roof made pine-like, to a point defined.
10056{_}2645{_}Fraternal love here bears its precious fruits,     And unfraternal schemes be ne'er designed!
10056{_}2646{_}Ancestral sway is his.
10056{_}2647{_}The walls they rear,     Five thousand cubits long; and south and west   The doors are placed.
10056{_}2648{_}Here will the king appear,     Here laugh, here talk, here sit him down and rest.
10056{_}2649{_}To mould the walls, the frames they firmly tie;     The toiling builders beat the earth and lime.
10056{_}2650{_}The walls shall vermin, storm, and bird defy;--     Fit dwelling is it for his lordly prime.
10056{_}2651{_}Grand is the hall the noble lord ascends;--     In height, like human form most reverent, grand;   And straight, as flies the shaft when bow unbends;     Its tints, like hues when pheasant's wings expand.
10056{_}2652{_}High pillars rise the level court around;     The pleasant light the open chamber steeps;   And deep recesses, wide alcoves, are found,     Where our good king in perfect quiet sleeps.
10056{_}2653{_}Laid is the bamboo mat on rush mat square;--     Here shall he sleep, and, waking, say, "Divine     What dreams are good?
10056{_}2654{_}For bear and grizzly bear,     And snakes and cobras, haunt this couch of mine."
10056{_}2655{_}Then shall the chief diviner glad reply,     "The bears foreshow that Heaven will send you sons.
10056{_}2656{_}The snakes and cobras daughters prophesy.
10056{_}2657{_}These auguries are all auspicious ones.
10056{_}2658{_}"Sons shall be his--on couches lulled to rest.
10056{_}2659{_}The little ones, enrobed, with sceptres play;   Their infant cries are loud as stern behest;     Their knees the vermeil covers shall display.
10056{_}2660{_}As king hereafter one shall be addressed;     The rest, our princes, all the States shall sway.
10056{_}2661{_}"And daughters also to him shall be born.
10056{_}2662{_}They shall be placed upon the ground to sleep;   Their playthings tiles, their dress the simplest worn;     Their part alike from good and ill to keep,   And ne'er their parents' hearts to cause to mourn;     To cook the food, and spirit-malt to steep."
10056{_}2663{_}~The Condition of King Seuen's Flocks~    Who dares to say your sheep are few?
10056{_}2664{_}The flocks are all three hundred strong.
10056{_}2665{_}Who dares despise your cattle too?
10056{_}2666{_}There ninety, black-lipped, press along.
10056{_}2667{_}Though horned the sheep, yet peaceful each appears;   The cattle come with moist and flapping ears.
10056{_}2668{_}These climb the heights, those drink the pool;     Some lie at rest, while others roam.
10056{_}2669{_}With rain-coats, and thin splint hats cool,     And bearing food, your herdsmen come.
10056{_}2670{_}In thirties, ranged by hues, the creatures stand;   Fit victims they will yield at your command.
10056{_}2671{_}Your herdsmen twigs and fagots bring,     With prey of birds and beasts for food.
10056{_}2672{_}Your sheep, untouched by evil thing,     Approach, their health and vigor good.
10056{_}2673{_}The herdsman's waving hand they all behold,   And docile come, and pass into the fold.
10056{_}2674{_}Your herdsmen dream;--fish take the place     Of men; on banners falcons fly,   Displacing snakes and tortoises.
10056{_}2675{_}The augur tells his prophecy:--   "The first betoken plenteous years; the change   Of banners shows of homes a widening range."
10056{_}2676{_}BOOK V    THE DECADE OF SEAOU MIN    ~A Eunuch Complains of His Fate~     A few fine lines, at random drawn,   Like the shell-pattern wrought in lawn     To hasty glance will seem.
10056{_}2677{_}My trivial faults base slander's slime   Distorted into foulest crime,     And men me worthless deem.
10056{_}2678{_}A few small points, pricked down on wood,   May be made out a picture good     Of the bright Southern Sieve.
10056{_}2679{_}Who planned, and helped those slanderers vile,   My name with base lies to defile?
10056{_}2680{_}Unpitied, here I grieve.
10056{_}2681{_}With babbling tongues you go about,   And only scheme how to make out     The lies you scatter round.
10056{_}2682{_}Hear me--Be careful what you say;   People ere long your words will weigh,     And liars you'll be found.
10056{_}2683{_}Clever you are with changeful schemes!
10056{_}2684{_}How else could all your evil dreams     And slanders work their way?
10056{_}2685{_}Men now believe you; by and by,   The truth found out, each vicious lie     Will ill for ill repay.
10056{_}2686{_}The proud rejoice; the sufferer weeps.
10056{_}2687{_}O azure Heaven, from out thy deeps     Why look in silence down?
10056{_}2688{_}Behold those proud men and rebuke;   With pity on the sufferers look,     And on the evil frown.
10056{_}2689{_}Those slanderers I would gladly take,   With all who help their schemes to make,     And to the tigers throw.
10056{_}2690{_}If wolves and tigers such should spare,   Td hurl them 'midst the freezing air,     Where the keen north winds blow.
10056{_}2691{_}And should the North compassion feel   I'd fling them to great Heaven, to deal     On them its direst woe.
10056{_}2692{_}As on the sacred heights you dwell,   My place is in the willow dell,     One is the other near.
10056{_}2693{_}Before you, officers, I spread   These lines by me, poor eunuch, made.
10056{_}2694{_}Think not Mang-tsze severe.
10056{_}2695{_}~An Officer Deplores the Misery of the Time~    In the fourth month summer shines;   In the sixth the heat declines.
10056{_}2696{_}Nature thus grants men relief;   Tyranny gives only grief.
10056{_}2697{_}Were not my forefathers men?
10056{_}2698{_}Can my suffering 'scape their ken?
10056{_}2699{_}In the cold of autumn days   Each plant shrivels and decays.
10056{_}2700{_}Nature then is hard and stern;   Living things sad lessons learn.
10056{_}2701{_}Friends dispersed, all order gone,   Place of refuge have I none.
10056{_}2702{_}Winter days are wild and fierce;   Rapid gusts each crevice pierce.
10056{_}2703{_}Such is my unhappy lot,   Unbefriended and forgot!
10056{_}2704{_}Others all can happy be;   I from misery ne'er am free.
10056{_}2705{_}On the mountains are fine trees;   Chestnuts, plum-trees, there one sees.
10056{_}2706{_}All the year their forms they show;   Stately more and more they grow.
10056{_}2707{_}Noble turned to ravening thief!
10056{_}2708{_}What the cause?
10056{_}2709{_}This stirs my grief.
10056{_}2710{_}Waters from that spring appear   Sometimes foul, and sometimes clear,   Changing oft as falls the rain,   Or the sky grows bright again.
10056{_}2711{_}New misfortunes every day   Still befall me, misery's prey.
10056{_}2712{_}Aid from mighty streams obtained,   Southern States are shaped and drained.
10056{_}2713{_}Thus the Keang and Han are thanked,   And as benefactors ranked.
10056{_}2714{_}Weary toil my vigor drains;   All unnoticed it remains!
10056{_}2715{_}Hawks and eagles mount the sky;   Sturgeons in deep waters lie.
10056{_}2716{_}Out of reach, they safely get,   Arrow fear not, nor the net.
10056{_}2717{_}Hiding-place for me there's none;   Here I stay, and make my moan.
10056{_}2718{_}Ferns upon the hills abound;   _Ke_ and _e_ in marshy ground.
10056{_}2719{_}Each can boast its proper place,   Where it grows for use or grace.
10056{_}2720{_}I can only sing the woe,   Which, ill-starred, I undergo.
10056{_}2721{_}~On the Alienation of a Friend~     Gently and soft the east wind blows,     And then there falls the pelting rain.
10056{_}2722{_}When anxious fears pressed round you close,     Then linked together were we twain.
10056{_}2723{_}Now happy, and your mind at rest,   You turn and cast me from your breast.
10056{_}2724{_}Gently and soft the east wind blows,     And then there comes the whirlwind wild.
10056{_}2725{_}When anxious fears pressed round you close,     Your bosom held me as a child.
10056{_}2726{_}Now happy, and in peaceful state,   You throw me off and quite forget.
10056{_}2727{_}Gently and soft the east wind blows,     Then round the rocky height it storms.
10056{_}2728{_}Each plant its leaves all dying shows;     The trees display their withered forms.
10056{_}2729{_}My virtues great forgotten all,   You keep in mind my faults, though small.
10056{_}2730{_}BOOK VI   THE DECADE OF PIH SHAN    ~A Picture of Husbandry~    Various the toils which fields so large demand!
10056{_}2731{_}We choose the seed; we take our tools in hand.
10056{_}2732{_}In winter for our work we thus prepare;   Then in the spring, bearing the sharpened 'share,   We to the acres go that south incline,   And to the earth the different seeds consign.
10056{_}2733{_}Soon, straight and large, upward each plant aspires;--   All happens as our noble lord desires.
10056{_}2734{_}The plants will ear; within their sheath confined,   The grains will harden, and be good in kind.
10056{_}2735{_}Nor darnel these, nor wolf's-tail grass infests;   From core and leaf we pick the insect pests,   And pick we those that eat the joints and roots:--   So do we guard from harm the growing fruits.
10056{_}2736{_}May the great Spirit, whom each farmer names,   Those insects take, and cast them to the flames!
10056{_}2737{_}The clouds o'erspread the sky in masses dense,   And gentle rain down to the earth dispense.
10056{_}2738{_}First may the public fields the blessing get,   And then with it our private fields we wet!
10056{_}2739{_}Patches of unripe grain the reaper leaves;   And here and there ungathered are the sheaves.
10056{_}2740{_}Handfuls besides we drop upon the ground,   And ears untouched in numbers lie around;--    These by the poor and widows shall be found.
10056{_}2741{_}When wives and children to the toilers come,   Bringing provisions from each separate home,   Our lord of long descent shall oft appear;   The Inspector also, glad the men to cheer.
10056{_}2742{_}They too shall thank the Spirits of the air,   With sacrifices pure for all their care;   Now red, now black, the victims that they slay,   As North or South the sacrifice they pay;   While millet bright the altars always show;--   And we shall thus still greater blessings know.
10056{_}2743{_}~The Complaint of an Officer~    O Heaven above, before whose light   Revealed is every deed and thought,      To thee I cry.
10056{_}2744{_}Hither on toilsome service brought,   In this wild K'ew I watch time's flight,      And sadly sigh.
10056{_}2745{_}The second month had just begun,   When from the east we took our way.
10056{_}2746{_}Through summer hot   We passed, and many a wintry day.
10056{_}2747{_}Summer again its course has run.
10056{_}2748{_}O bitter lot!
10056{_}2749{_}There are my compeers, gay at court,   While here the tears my face begrime.
10056{_}2750{_}I'd fain return--   But there is that dread net for crime!
10056{_}2751{_}The fear of it the wish cuts short.
10056{_}2752{_}In vain I burn!
10056{_}2753{_}Ere we the royal city left,   The sun and moon renewed the year.
10056{_}2754{_}We marched in hope.
10056{_}2755{_}Now to its close this year is near.
10056{_}2756{_}Return deferred, of hope bereft,      All mourn and mope.
10056{_}2757{_}My lonesome state haunts aye my breast,   While duties grow, and cares increase,      Too hard to bear.
10056{_}2758{_}Toils that oppress me never cease;   Not for a moment dare I rest,     Nigh to despair.
10056{_}2759{_}I think with fond regard of those,   Who in their posts at court remain,     My friends of old.
10056{_}2760{_}Fain would I be with them again,   But fierce reproof return would cause.
10056{_}2761{_}This post I hold.
10056{_}2762{_}When for the West I left my home,   The sun and moon both mildly shone,     Our hearts to cheer.
10056{_}2763{_}We'd soon be back, our service done!
10056{_}2764{_}Alas!
10056{_}2765{_}affairs more urgent come,     And fix us here.
10056{_}2766{_}The year is hastening to expire.
10056{_}2767{_}We gather now the southern-wood,     The beans we reap;--   That for its fragrance, these for food.
10056{_}2768{_}Such things that constant care require     Me anxious keep.
10056{_}2769{_}Thinking of friends still at their posts,   I rise and pass the night outside,     So vexed my mind.
10056{_}2770{_}But soon what changes may betide?
10056{_}2771{_}I here will stay, whate'er it costs,     And be resigned.
10056{_}2772{_}My honored friends, O do not deem   Your rest which seems secure from ill     Will ever last!
10056{_}2773{_}Your duties quietly fulfil,   And hold the upright in esteem,     With friendship fast.
10056{_}2774{_}So shall the Spirits hear your cry,   You virtuous make, and good supply,     In measure vast.
10056{_}2775{_}My honored friends, O do not deem   Repose that seems secure from ill     Will lasting prove.
10056{_}2776{_}Your duties quietly fulfil,   And hold the upright in esteem,     With earnest love.
10056{_}2777{_}So shall the Spirits hear your prayer,   And on you happiness confer,     Your hopes above.
10056{_}2778{_}BOOK VII    DECADE OF SANG HOO    ~The Rejoicings of a Bridegroom~    With axle creaking, all on fire I went,    To fetch my young and lovely bride.
10056{_}2779{_}No thirst or hunger pangs my bosom rent--    I only longed to have her by my side.
10056{_}2780{_}I feast with her, whose virtue fame had told,   Nor need we friends our rapture to behold.
10056{_}2781{_}The long-tailed pheasants surest covert find,    Amid the forest on the plain.
10056{_}2782{_}Here from my virtuous bride, of noble mind,    And person tall, I wisdom gain.
10056{_}2783{_}I praise her while we feast, and to her say,   "The love I bear you ne'er will know decay.
10056{_}2784{_}"Poor we may be; spirits and viands fine    My humble means will not afford.
10056{_}2785{_}But what we have, we'll taste and not repine;    From us will come no grumbling word.
10056{_}2786{_}And though to you no virtue I can add,   Yet we will sing and dance, in spirit glad.
10056{_}2787{_}"I oft ascend that lofty ridge with toil,    And hew large branches from the oaks;   Then of their leafy glory them I spoil,    And fagots form with vigorous strokes.
10056{_}2788{_}Returning tired, your matchless grace I see,   And my whole soul dissolves in ecstasy.
10056{_}2789{_}"To the high hills I looked, and urged each steed;    The great road next was smooth and plain.
10056{_}2790{_}Up hill, o'er dale, I never slackened speed;     Like lute-string sounded every rein.
10056{_}2791{_}I knew, my journey ended, I should come   To you, sweet bride, the comfort of my home."
10056{_}2792{_}~Against Listening to Slanderers~    Like the blueflies buzzing round,     And on the fences lighting,   Are the sons of slander found,     Who never cease their biting.
10056{_}2793{_}O thou happy, courteous king,   To the winds their slanders fling.
10056{_}2794{_}Buzzing round the blueflies hear,     About the jujubes flocking!
10056{_}2795{_}So the slanderers appear,     Whose calumnies are shocking.
10056{_}2796{_}By no law or order bound,   All the kingdom they confound.
10056{_}2797{_}How they buzz, those odious flies,     Upon the hazels clust'ring!
10056{_}2798{_}And as odious are the lies     Of those slanderers blust'ring.
10056{_}2799{_}Hatred stirred between us two   Shows the evil they can do.
10056{_}2800{_}BOOK VIII    THE DECADE OF TOO JIN SZE    ~In Praise of By-gone Simplicity~     In the old capital they stood,     With yellow fox-furs plain,   Their manners all correct and good,     Speech free from vulgar stain.
10056{_}2801{_}Could we go back to Chow's old days,   All would look up to them with praise.
10056{_}2802{_}In the old capital they wore     _T'ae_ hats and black caps small;   And ladies, who famed surnames bore,     Their own thick hair let fall.
10056{_}2803{_}Such simple ways are seen no more,   And the changed manners I deplore.
10056{_}2804{_}Ear-rings, made of plainest gold,     In the old days were worn.
10056{_}2805{_}Each lady of a noble line     A Yin or Keih seemed born.
10056{_}2806{_}Such officers and ladies now   I see not and my sorrows grow.
10056{_}2807{_}With graceful sweep their girdles fell,     Then in the days of old.
10056{_}2808{_}The ladies' side-hair, with a swell,     Like scorpion's tail, rose bold.
10056{_}2809{_}Such, if I saw them in these days,   I'd follow with admiring gaze.
10056{_}2810{_}So hung their girdles, not for show;--     To their own length 'twas due.
10056{_}2811{_}'Twas not by art their hair curled so;--     By nature so it grew.
10056{_}2812{_}I seek such manners now in vain,   And pine for them with longing pain.
10056{_}2813{_}[NOTE.--Yin and Keih were clan names of great families, the ladies of which would be leaders of fashion in the capital.]
10056{_}2814{_}~A Wife Bemoans Her Husband's Absence~    So full am I of anxious thought,   Though all the morn king-grass I've sought,     To fill my arms I fail.
10056{_}2815{_}Like wisp all-tangled is my hair!
10056{_}2816{_}To wash it let me home repair.
10056{_}2817{_}My lord soon may I hail!
10056{_}2818{_}Though 'mong the indigo I've wrought   The morning long; through anxious thought     My skirt's filled but in part.
10056{_}2819{_}Within five days he was to appear;   The sixth has come and he's not here.
10056{_}2820{_}Oh!
10056{_}2821{_}how this racks my heart!
10056{_}2822{_}When here we dwelt in union sweet,   If the hunt called his eager feet,     His bow I cased for him.
10056{_}2823{_}Or if to fish he went away,   And would be absent all the day,     His line I put in trim.
10056{_}2824{_}What in his angling did he catch?
10056{_}2825{_}Well worth the time it was to watch     How bream and tench he took.
10056{_}2826{_}Men thronged upon the banks and gazed;   At bream and tench they looked amazed,     The triumphs of his hook.
10056{_}2827{_}~The Earl of Shaou's Work~    As the young millet, by the genial rain     Enriched, shoots up luxuriant and tall,   So, when we southward marched with toil and pain,     The Earl of Shaou cheered and inspired us all.
10056{_}2828{_}We pushed our barrows, and our burdens bore;     We drove our wagons, and our oxen led.
10056{_}2829{_}"The work once done, our labor there is o'er,     And home we travel," to ourselves we said.
10056{_}2830{_}Close kept our footmen round the chariot track;     Our eager host in close battalions sped.
10056{_}2831{_}"When once our work is done, then we go back,     Our labor over," to themselves they said.
10056{_}2832{_}Hard was the work we had at Seay to do,     But Shaou's great earl the city soon upreared.
10056{_}2833{_}The host its service gave with ardor true;--     Such power in all the earl's commands appeared!
10056{_}2834{_}We did on plains and low lands what was meet;     We cleared the springs and streams, the land to drain.
10056{_}2835{_}The Earl of Shaou announced his work complete,     And the King's heart reposed, at rest again.
10056{_}2836{_}~The Plaint of King Yew's Forsaken Wife~    The fibres of the white-flowered rush     Are with the white grass bound.
10056{_}2837{_}So do the two together go,     In closest union found.
10056{_}2838{_}And thus should man and wife abide,     The twain combined in one;   But this bad man sends me away,     And bids me dwell alone.
10056{_}2839{_}Both rush and grass from the bright clouds     The genial dew partake.
10056{_}2840{_}Kind and impartial, nature's laws     No odious difference make.
10056{_}2841{_}But providence appears unkind;     Events are often hard.
10056{_}2842{_}This man, to principle untrue,     Denies me his regard.
10056{_}2843{_}Northward the pools their waters send,     To flood each paddy field;   So get the fields the sap they need,     Their store of rice to yield.
10056{_}2844{_}But that great man no deed of grace     Deigns to bestow on me.
10056{_}2845{_}My songs are sighs.
10056{_}2846{_}At thought of him     My heart aches wearily.
10056{_}2847{_}The mulberry branches they collect,     And use their food to cook;   But I must use a furnace small,     That pot nor pan will brook.
10056{_}2848{_}So me that great man badly treats,     Nor uses as his wife,   Degrades me from my proper place,     And fills with grief my life.
10056{_}2849{_}The bells and drums inside the court     Men stand without and hear;   So should the feelings in my breast,     To him distinct appear.
10056{_}2850{_}All-sorrowful, I think of him,     Longing to move his love;   But he vouchsafes no kind response;     His thoughts far from me rove.
10056{_}2851{_}The marabow stands on the dam,     And to repletion feeds;   The crane deep in the forest cries,     Nor finds the food it needs.
10056{_}2852{_}So in my room the concubine     By the great man is placed;   While I with cruel banishment     Am cast out and disgraced.
10056{_}2853{_}The yellow ducks sit on the dam,      With left wing gathered low;   So on each other do they lean,      And their attachment show.
10056{_}2854{_}And love should thus the man and wife      In closest concord bind;   But that man turns away from me,      And shows a fickle mind.
10056{_}2855{_}When one stands on a slab of stone,      No higher than the ground,   Nothing is added to his height;--      Low with the stone he's found.
10056{_}2856{_}So does the favorite's mean estate      Render that great man mean,   While I by him, to distance sent,      Am pierced with sorrow keen.
10056{_}2857{_}~Hospitality~    A few gourd leaves that waved about      Cut down and boiled;--the feast how spare!
10056{_}2858{_}But the good host his spirits takes,      Pours out a cup, and proves them rare.
10056{_}2859{_}A single rabbit on the mat,      Or baked, or roast:--how small the feast!
10056{_}2860{_}But the good host his spirits takes,      And fills the cup of every guest.
10056{_}2861{_}A single rabbit on the mat,      Roasted or broiled:--how poor the meal!
10056{_}2862{_}But the guests from the spirit vase      Fill their host's cup, and drink his weal.
10056{_}2863{_}A single rabbit on the mat,      Roasted or baked:--no feast we think!
10056{_}2864{_}But from the spirit vase they take,      Both host and guests, and joyous drink.
10056{_}2865{_}~On the Misery of Soldiers~    Yellow now is all the grass;   All the days in marching pass.
10056{_}2866{_}On the move is every man;   Hard work, far and near, they plan.
10056{_}2867{_}Black is every plant become;   Every man is torn from home.
10056{_}2868{_}Kept on foot, our state is sad;--   As if we no feelings had!
10056{_}2869{_}Not rhinoceroses we!
10056{_}2870{_}Tigers do we care to be?
10056{_}2871{_}Fields like these so desolate   Are to us a hateful fate.
10056{_}2872{_}Long-tailed foxes pleased may hide   'Mong the grass, where they abide.
10056{_}2873{_}We, in box carts slowly borne,   On the great roads plod and mourn.
10056{_}2874{_}PART III.--GREATER ODES OF THE KINGDOM    BOOK I    DECADE OF KING WAN   ~Celebrating King Wan~    The royal Wan now rests on high,   Enshrined in brightness of the sky.
10056{_}2875{_}Chow as a state had long been known,   And Heaven's decree at last was shown.
10056{_}2876{_}Its lords had borne a glorious name;   God kinged them when the season came.
10056{_}2877{_}King Wan ruled well when earth he trod;   Now moves his spirit near to God.
10056{_}2878{_}A strong-willed, earnest king was Wan,   And still his fame rolls widening on.
10056{_}2879{_}The gifts that God bestowed on Chow   Belong to Wan's descendants now.
10056{_}2880{_}Heaven blesses still with gifts divine   The hundred scions of his line;   And all the officers of Chow   From age to age more lustrous grow.
10056{_}2881{_}More lustrous still from age to age,   All reverent plans their zeal engage;   And brilliant statesmen owe their birth   To this much-favored spot of earth.
10056{_}2882{_}They spring like products of the land--   The men by whom the realm doth stand.
10056{_}2883{_}Such aid their numerous bands supply,   That Wan rests tranquilly on high.
10056{_}2884{_}Deep were Wan's thoughts, sustained his ways;   His reverence lit its trembling rays.
10056{_}2885{_}Resistless came great Heaven's decree;   The sons of Shang must bend the knee;--   The sons of Shang, each one a king,   In numbers beyond numbering.
10056{_}2886{_}Yet as God spoke, so must it be:--   The sons of Shang all bent the knee.
10056{_}2887{_}Now each to Chow his homage pays--   So dark and changing are Heaven's ways.
10056{_}2888{_}When we pour our libations here,   The officers of Shang appear,   Quick and alert to give their aid:--   Such is the service by them paid,   While still they do not cast aside   The cap and broidered axe--their pride.
10056{_}2889{_}Ye servants of our line of kings,   Remember him from whom it springs.
10056{_}2890{_}Remember him from whom it springs;--   Let this give to your virtue wings.
10056{_}2891{_}Seek harmony with Heaven's great mind;--   So shall you surest blessing find.
10056{_}2892{_}Ere Shang had lost the nation's heart,   Its monarchs all with God had part   In sacrifice.
10056{_}2893{_}From them you see   'Tis hard to keep high Heaven's decree.
10056{_}2894{_}'Tis hard to keep high Heaven's decree!
10056{_}2895{_}O sin not, or you cease to be.
10056{_}2896{_}To add true lustre to your name,   See Shang expire in Heaven's dread flame.
10056{_}2897{_}For Heaven's high dealings are profound,   And far transcend all sense and sound.
10056{_}2898{_}From Wan your pattern you must draw,   And all the States will own your law.

10056{_}2900{_}is omitted]   BOOK III [*]    DECADE OF TANG    ~King Seuen on the Occasion of a Great Drought~    Grand shone the Milky Way on high,   With brilliant span athwart the sky,     Nor promise gave of rain.
10056{_}2901{_}King Seuen long gazed; then from him broke,   In anguished tones the words he spoke.
10056{_}2902{_}Well might he thus complain!
10056{_}2903{_}"O Heaven, what crimes have we to own,   That death and ruin still come down?
10056{_}2904{_}Relentless famine fills our graves.
10056{_}2905{_}Pity the king who humbly craves!
10056{_}2906{_}Our miseries never cease.
10056{_}2907{_}To every Spirit I have vowed;   The choicest victim's blood has flowed.
10056{_}2908{_}As offerings I have freely paid   My store of gems and purest jade.
10056{_}2909{_}Hear me, and give release!
10056{_}2910{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2911{_}As on wing   Its fervors fly, and torment bring.
10056{_}2912{_}With purest mind and ceaseless care   My sacrifices I prepare.
10056{_}2913{_}At thine own border altars, Heaven,   And in my father's fane, I've given     What might relief have found.
10056{_}2914{_}What Powers above, below, have sway,   To all my precious gifts I pay,     Then bury in the ground.
10056{_}2915{_}Yes, every Spirit has received   Due honor, and, still unrelieved,     Our sufferings greater grow.
10056{_}2916{_}How-tseih can't give the needed aid,   And help from God is still delayed!
10056{_}2917{_}The country lies a ruined waste.
10056{_}2918{_}O would that I alone might taste     This bitter cup of woe!
10056{_}2919{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2920{_}Nor do I   To fix the blame on others try.
10056{_}2921{_}I quake with dread; the risk I feel,   As when I hear the thunders peal,     Or fear its sudden crash.
10056{_}2922{_}Our black-haired race, a remnant now,   Will every one be swept from Chow,     As by the lightning's flash.
10056{_}2923{_}Nor I myself will live alone.
10056{_}2924{_}God from his great and heavenly throne     Will not spare even me.
10056{_}2925{_}O friends and officers, come, blend   Your prayers with mine; come, lowly bend.
10056{_}2926{_}Chow's dynasty will pass away;   Its altars at no distant day     In ruins all shall be!
10056{_}2927{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2928{_}It keeps on   Its fatal course.
10056{_}2929{_}All hope is gone.
10056{_}2930{_}The air more fierce and fiery glows.
10056{_}2931{_}Where can I fly?
10056{_}2932{_}Where seek repose?
10056{_}2933{_}Death marks me for its prey.
10056{_}2934{_}Above, no saving hand!
10056{_}2935{_}Around,   No hope, no comfort, can be found.
10056{_}2936{_}The dukes and ministers of old   Give us no help.
10056{_}2937{_}Can ye withhold   Your sympathy, who lately reigned?
10056{_}2938{_}And parents, how are you restrained,     In this so dreadful day?
10056{_}2939{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2940{_}There on high   The hills are parched.
10056{_}2941{_}The streams are dry.
10056{_}2942{_}Drought's demon stalks abroad in ire,   And scatters wide his flames and fire.
10056{_}2943{_}Alas, my woful heart!
10056{_}2944{_}The fires within its strength consume;   The heat without creates a gloom     That from it will not part.
10056{_}2945{_}The dukes and ministers by-gone   Respond not to my prayer and moan.
10056{_}2946{_}God in great Heaven, permission give   That I may in retirement live,     And try to heal my smart!
10056{_}2947{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2948{_}Still I strive,   And will not leave while I survive.
10056{_}2949{_}Duty to shun I fear.
10056{_}2950{_}Why upon me has come this drought?
10056{_}2951{_}Vainly I try to search it out,     Vainly, with quest severe.
10056{_}2952{_}For a good harvest soon I prayed,   Nor late the rites I duly paid,   To Spirits of the air and land.
10056{_}2953{_}There wanted nought they could demand,     Their favor to secure.
10056{_}2954{_}God in great heaven, be just, be kind!
10056{_}2955{_}Thou dost not bear me in Thy mind.
10056{_}2956{_}My cry, ye wisest Spirits, hear!
10056{_}2957{_}Ye whom I constantly revere,     Why do I this endure?
10056{_}2958{_}"The drought consumes us.
10056{_}2959{_}People fly,   And leave their homes.
10056{_}2960{_}Each social tie     And bond of rule is snapt.
10056{_}2961{_}The Heads of Boards are all perplexed;   My premier's mind is sorely vexed;     In trouble all are wrapt.
10056{_}2962{_}The Masters of my Horse and Guards;   My cook, and men of different wards:--   Not one has from the struggle shrunk.
10056{_}2963{_}Though feeling weak, they have not sunk,     But done their best to aid.
10056{_}2964{_}To the great sky I look with pain;--   Why do these grievous sorrows rain     On my devoted head?
10056{_}2965{_}"Yes, at the mighty sky I gaze,   And lo!
10056{_}2966{_}the stars pursue their maze,     And sparkle clear and bright.
10056{_}2967{_}Ah!
10056{_}2968{_}Heaven nor helps, nor seems to ken.
10056{_}2969{_}Great officers and noble men,   With all your powers ye well have striven,   And reverently have sought from Heaven     Its aid in our great fight.
10056{_}2970{_}My death is near; but oh!
10056{_}2971{_}keep on,   And do as thus far you have done.
10056{_}2972{_}Regard you only me?
10056{_}2973{_}No, for yourselves and all your friends,   On whom for rule the land depends,     You seek security.
10056{_}2974{_}I turn my gaze to the great sky;--   When shall this drought be done, and I     Quiet and restful be?"
10056{_}2975{_}[NOTE *: Selections from Book II.
10056{_}2976{_}are omitted.--EDITOR.]
10056{_}2977{_}PART IV.--ODES OF THE TEMPLE AND ALTAR    BOOK I    SACRIFICIAL ODES OF CHOW    ~Appropriate to a Sacrifice to King Wan~    My offerings here are given,     A ram, a bull.
10056{_}2978{_}Accept them, mighty Heaven,     All-bountiful.
10056{_}2979{_}Thy statutes, O great king,     I keep, I love;   So on the realm to bring     Peace from above.
10056{_}2980{_}From Wan comes blessing rich;     Now on the right   He owns those gifts to which     Him I invite.
10056{_}2981{_}Do I not night and day,     Revere great Heaven,   That thus its favor may     To Chow be given?
10056{_}2982{_}~On Sacrificing to the Kings Woo, Ching, and K'ang~    The arm of Woo was full of might;     None could his fire withstand;   And Ching and K'ang stood forth to sight,     As kinged by God's own hand.
10056{_}2983{_}We err not when we call them sage.
10056{_}2984{_}How grandly they maintained   Their hold of all the heritage     That Wan and Woo had gained!
10056{_}2985{_}As here we worship, they descend,     While bells and drums resound,   And stones and lutes their music blend.
10056{_}2986{_}With blessings we are crowned.
10056{_}2987{_}The rites correctly we discharge;     The feast we freely share.
10056{_}2988{_}Those Sires Chow's glory will enlarge,     And ever for it care.
10056{_}2989{_}THE TRAVELS OF FA-HIEN    [Translation by James Legge]   TRANSLATOR'S INTRODUCTION   Nothing of great importance is known about Fa-hien in addition to what may be gathered from his own record of his travels.
10056{_}2990{_}I have read the accounts of him in the "Memoirs of Eminent Monks," compiled in A.D. 519, and a later work, the "Memoirs of Marvellous Monks," by the third emperor of the Ming dynasty (A.D. 1403-1424), which, however, is nearly all borrowed from the other; and all in them that has an appearance of verisimilitude can be brought within brief compass.
10056{_}2991{_}His surname, they tell us, was Kung, and he was a native of Wu-yang in P'ing-yang, which is still the name of a large department in Shan-hsi.
10056{_}2992{_}He had three brothers older than himself; but when they all died before shedding their first teeth, his father devoted him to the service of the Buddhist society, and had him entered as a Sramanera, still keeping him at home in the family.
10056{_}2993{_}The little fellow fell dangerously ill, and the father sent him to the monastery, where he soon got well and refused to return to his parents.
10056{_}2994{_}When he was ten years old, his father died; and an uncle, considering the widowed solitariness and helplessness of the mother, urged him to renounce the monastic life, and return to her, but the boy replied, "I did not quit the family in compliance with my father's wishes, but because I wished to be far from the dust and vulgar ways of life.
10056{_}2995{_}This is why I choose monkhood."
10056{_}2996{_}The uncle approved of his words and gave over urging him.
10056{_}2997{_}When his mother also died, it appeared how great had been the affection for her of his fine nature; but after her burial he returned to the monastery.
10056{_}2998{_}On one occasion he was cutting rice with a score or two of his fellow-disciples, when some hungry thieves came upon them to take away their grain by force.
10056{_}2999{_}The other Sramaneras all fled, but our young hero stood his ground, and said to the thieves, "If you must have the grain, take what you please.
10056{_}3000{_}But, sirs, it was your former neglect of charity which brought you to your present state of destitution; and now, again, you wish to rob others.
10056{_}3001{_}I am afraid that in the coming ages you will have still greater poverty and distress; I am sorry for you beforehand."
10056{_}3002{_}With these words he followed his companions to the monastery, while the thieves left the grain and went away, all the monks, of whom there were several hundred, doing homage to his conduct and courage.
10056{_}3003{_}When he had finished his novitiate and taken on him the obligations of the full Buddhist orders, his earnest courage, clear intelligence, and strict regulation of his demeanor, were conspicuous; and soon after, he undertook his journey to India in search of complete copies of the Vinaya-pitaka.
10056{_}3004{_}What follows this is merely an account of his travels in India and return to China by sea, condensed from his own narrative, with the addition of some marvellous incidents that happened to him, on his visit to the Vulture Peak near Rajagriha.
10056{_}3005{_}It is said in the end that after his return to China, he went to the capital (evidently Nanking), and there, along with the Indian Sramana Buddha-bhadra, executed translations of some of the works which he had obtained in India; and that before he had done all that he wished to do in this way, he removed to King-chow (in the present Hoo-pih), and died in the monastery of Sin, at the age of eighty-eight, to the great sorrow of all who knew him.
10056{_}3006{_}It is added that there is another larger work giving an account of his travels in various countries.
10056{_}3007{_}Such is all the information given about our author, beyond what he has himself told us.
10056{_}3008{_}Fa-hien was his clerical name, and means "Illustrious in the Law," or "Illustrious master of the Law."
10056{_}3009{_}The Shih which often precedes it is an abbreviation of the name of Buddha as Sakyamuni, "the Sakya, mighty in Love, dwelling in Seclusion and Silence," and may be taken as equivalent to Buddhist.
10056{_}3010{_}He is sometimes said to have belonged to "the eastern Tsin dynasty" (A.D. 317-419), and sometimes to "the Sung," that is, the Sung dynasty of the House of Liu (A.D. 420-478).
10056{_}3011{_}If he became a full monk at the age of twenty, and went to India when he was twenty-five, his long life may have been divided pretty equally between the two dynasties.
10056{_}3012{_}If there were ever another and larger account of Fa-hien's travels than the narrative of which a translation is now given, it has long ceased to be in existence.
10056{_}3013{_}In the catalogue of the imperial library of the Suy dynasty (A.D. 589-618), the name Fa-hien occurs four times.
10056{_}3014{_}Towards the end of the last section of it, after a reference to his travels, his labors in translation at Kin-ling (another name for Nanking), in conjunction with Buddha-bhadra, are described.
10056{_}3015{_}In the second section we find "A Record of Buddhistic Kingdoms"--with a note, saying that it was the work of "the Sramana, Fa-hien"; and again, we have "Narrative of Fa-hien in two Books," and "Narrative of Fa-hien's Travels in one Book."
10056{_}3016{_}But all these three entries may possibly belong to different copies of the same work, the first and the other two being in separate subdivisions of the catalogue.
10056{_}3017{_}In the two Chinese copies of the narrative in my possession the title is "Record of Buddhistic Kingdoms."
10056{_}3018{_}In the Japanese or Corean recension the title is twofold; first, "Narrative of the Distinguished Monk, Fa-hien"; and then, more at large, "Incidents of Travels in India, by the Sramana of the Eastern Tsin, Fa-hien, recorded by himself."
10056{_}3019{_}There is still earlier attestation of the existence of our little work than the Suy catalogue.
10056{_}3020{_}The "Catalogue Raisonne" of the imperial library of the present dynasty mentions two quotations from it by Le Tao-yueen, a geographical writer of the dynasty of the Northern Wei (A.D. 386-584), one of them containing eighty-nine characters, and the other two hundred and seventy-six; both of them given as from the "Narrative of Fa-hien."
10056{_}3021{_}In all catalogues subsequent to that of Suy our work appears.
10056{_}3022{_}The evidence for its authenticity and genuineness is all that could be required.
10056{_}3023{_}It is clear to myself that the "Record of Buddhistic Kingdoms" and the "Narrative of his Travels by Fa-hien" were designations of one and the same work, and that it is doubtful whether any larger work on the same subject was ever current.
10056{_}3024{_}With regard to the text subjoined to my translation, it was published in Japan in 1779.
10056{_}3025{_}The editor had before him four recensions of the narrative; those of the Sung and Ming dynasties, with appendices on the names of certain characters in them; that of Japan; and that of Corea.
10056{_}3026{_}He wisely adopted the Corean text, published in accordance with a royal rescript in 1726, so far as I can make out; but the different readings of the other texts are all given in top-notes, instead of foot-notes as with us, this being one of the points in which customs in the East and West go by contraries.
10056{_}3027{_}Very occasionally, the editor indicates by a single character, equivalent to "right" or "wrong," which reading in his opinion is to be preferred.
10056{_}3028{_}The editors of the "Catalogue Raisonne" intimate their doubts of the good taste and reliability of all Fa-hien's statements.
10056{_}3029{_}It offends them that he should call central India the "Middle Kingdom," and China, which to them was the true and only Middle Kingdom, but "a Border-land"--it offends them as the vaunting language of a Buddhist writer, whereas the reader will see in the expressions only an instance of what Fa-hien calls his "simple straightforwardness."
10056{_}3030{_}As an instance of his unreliability they refer to his account of the Buddhism of Khoten, whereas it is well-known, they say, that the Khoteners from ancient times till now have been Mohammedans;--as if they could have been so one hundred and seventy years before Mohammed was born, and two hundred twenty-two years before the year of the Hegira!
10056{_}3031{_}And this is criticism in China.
10056{_}3032{_}The catalogue was ordered by the K'ien-lung emperor in 1722.
10056{_}3033{_}Between three and four hundred of the "Great Scholars" of the empire were engaged on it in various departments, and thus egregiously ignorant did they show themselves of all beyond the limits of their own country, and even of the literature of that country itself.
10056{_}3034{_}Much of what Fa-hien tells his readers of Buddhist miracles and legends is indeed unreliable and grotesque; but we have from him the truth as to what he saw and heard.
10056{_}3035{_}In concluding this introduction I wish to call attention to some estimates of the number of Buddhists in the world which have become current, believing, as I do, that the smallest of them is much above what is correct.
10056{_}3036{_}In a note on the first page of his work on the Bhilsa Topes (1854), General Cunningham says: "The Christians number about two hundred and seventy millions; the Buddhists about two hundred and twenty-two millions, who are distributed as follows: China one hundred and seventy millions, Japan twenty-five millions, Anam fourteen millions, Siam three millions, Ava eight millions, Nepal one million, and Ceylon one million."
10056{_}3037{_}In his article on M.J. Barthelemy Saint-Hilaire's "Le Bouddha et sa Religion," republished in his "Chips from a German workshop," vol.
10056{_}3038{_}i.
10056{_}3039{_}(1868), Professor Max Mueller says, "The young prince became the founder of a religion which, after more than two thousand years, is still professed by four hundred and fifty-five millions of human beings," and he appends the following note: "Though truth is not settled by majorities, it would be interesting to know which religion counts at the present moment the largest numbers of believers.
10056{_}3040{_}Berghaus, in his 'Physical Atlas,' gives the following division of the human race according to religion: 'Buddhists 31.2 per cent., Christians 30.7, Mohammedans 15.7, Brahmanists 13.4, Heathens 8.7, and Jews O.3.'
10056{_}3041{_}As Berghaus does not distinguish the Buddhists in China from the followers of Confucius and Laotse, the first place on the scale belongs really to Christianity.
10056{_}3042{_}It is difficult in China to say to what religion a man belongs, as the same person may profess two or three.
10056{_}3043{_}The emperor himself, after sacrificing according to the ritual of Confucius, visits a Tao-tse temple, and afterwards bows before an image of Fo in a Buddhist chapel."
10056{_}3044{_}("Melanges Asiatiques de St. Petersbourg," vol.
10056{_}3045{_}ii.
10056{_}3046{_}p.
10056{_}3047{_}374.)
10056{_}3048{_}Both these estimates are exceeded by Dr. T.W.
10056{_}3049{_}Rhys Davids (intimating also the uncertainty of the statements, and that numbers are no evidence of truth) in the introduction to his "Manual of Buddhism."
10056{_}3050{_}The Buddhists there appear as amounting in all to five hundred millions:--thirty millions of Southern Buddhists, in Ceylon, Burma, Siam, Anam, and India (Jains); and four hundred and seventy millions of Northern Buddhists, of whom nearly thirty-three millions are assigned to Japan, and 414,686,974 to the eighteen provinces of China proper.
10056{_}3051{_}According to him, Christians amount to about 26 per cent, of mankind, Hindus to about 13, Mohammedans to about 12-1/2, Buddhists to about 40, and Jews to about one-half of one per cent.
10056{_}3052{_}In regard to all these estimates, it will be observed that the immense numbers assigned to Buddhism are made out by the multitude of Chinese with which it is credited.
10056{_}3053{_}Subtract Cunningham's one hundred and seventy millions of Chinese from his total of two hundred and twenty-two millions, and there remain only fifty-two millions of Buddhists.
10056{_}3054{_}Subtract Davids's four hundred fourteen and one-half millions of Chinese from his total of five hundred millions, and there remain only eighty-five and one-half millions for Buddhism.
10056{_}3055{_}Of the numbers assigned to other countries, as well as of their whole populations, I am in considerable doubt, excepting in the cases of Ceylon and India; but the greatness of the estimates turns upon the immense multitudes said to be in China.
10056{_}3056{_}I do not know what total population Cunningham allowed for that country, nor on what principle he allotted one hundred and seventy millions of it to Buddhism; perhaps he halved his estimate of the whole, whereas Berghaus and Davids allotted to it the highest estimates that have been given of the people.
10056{_}3057{_}But we have no certain information of the population of China.
10056{_}3058{_}At an interview with the former Chinese ambassador, Kwo Sung-tao, in Paris, in 1878, I begged him to write out for me the amount, with the authority for it, and he assured me that it could not be done.
10056{_}3059{_}I have read probably almost everything that has been published on the subject, and endeavored by methods of my own to arrive at a satisfactory conclusion;--without reaching a result which I can venture to lay before the public.
10056{_}3060{_}My impression has been that four hundred millions is hardly an exaggeration.
10056{_}3061{_}But supposing that we had reliable returns of the whole population, how shall we proceed to apportion that among Confucianists, Taoists, and Buddhists?
10056{_}3062{_}Confucianism is the orthodoxy of China.
10056{_}3063{_}The common name for it is Ju Chiao, "the Doctrines held by the Learned Class," entrance into the circle of which is, with a few insignificant exceptions, open to all the people.
10056{_}3064{_}The mass of them and the masses under their influence are preponderatingly Confucian; and in the observance of ancestral worship, the most remarkable feature of the religion proper of China from the earliest times, of which Confucius was not the author but the prophet, an overwhelming majority are regular and assiduous.
10056{_}3065{_}Among "the strange principles" which the emperor of the K'ang-hsi period, in one of his famous Sixteen Precepts, exhorted his people to "discountenance and put away, in order to exalt the correct doctrine," Buddhism and Taoism were both included.
10056{_}3066{_}If, as stated in the note quoted from Professor Mueller, the emperor countenances both the Taoist worship and the Buddhist, he does so for reasons of state; to please especially his Buddhistic subjects in Thibet and Mongolia, and not to offend the many whose superstitious fancies incline to Taoism.
10056{_}3067{_}When I went out and in as a missionary among the Chinese people for about thirty years, it sometimes occurred to me that only the inmates of their monasteries and the recluses of both systems should be enumerated as Buddhists and Taoists; but I was in the end constrained to widen that judgment, and to admit a considerable following of both among the people, who have neither received the tonsure nor assumed the yellow top.
10056{_}3068{_}Dr. Eitel, in concluding his discussion of this point in his "Lecture on Buddhism, an Event in History," says: "It is not too much to say that most Chinese are theoretically Confucianists, but emotionally Buddhists or Taoists.
10056{_}3069{_}But fairness requires us to add that, though the mass of the people are more or less influenced by Buddhist doctrines, yet the people, as a whole, have no respect for the Buddhist church, and habitually sneer at Buddhist priests."
10056{_}3070{_}For the "most" in the former of these two sentences I would substitute "nearly all;" and between my friend's "but" and "emotionally" I would introduce "many are," and would not care to contest his conclusion further.
10056{_}3071{_}It does seem to me preposterous to credit Buddhism with the whole of the vast population of China, the great majority of whom are Confucianists.
10056{_}3072{_}My own opinion is that its adherents are not so many as those even of Mohammedanism, and that instead of being the most numerous of the religions (so-called) of the world, it is only entitled to occupy the fifth place, ranking below Christianity, Confucianism, Brahmanism, and Mohammedanism, and followed, some distance off, by Taoism.
10056{_}3073{_}To make a table of percentages of mankind, and to assign to each system its proportion, are to seem to be wise where we are deplorably ignorant; and, moreover, if our means of information were much better than they are, our figures would merely show the outward adherence.
10056{_}3074{_}A fractional percentage might tell more for one system than a very large integral one for another.
10056{_}3075{_}JAMES LEGGE.
10056{_}3076{_}THE TRAVELS OF FA-HIEN    CHAPTER I  ~From Ch'ang-gan to the Sandy Desert~   Fa-Hien had been living in Ch'ang-gan.
10056{_}3077{_}[1] Deploring the mutilated and imperfect state of the collection of the Books of Discipline, in the second year of the period Hwang-che, being the Ke-hae year of the cycle, [2] he entered into an engagement with Hwuy-king, Tao-ching, Hwuy-ying, and Hwuy-wei, that they should go to India and seek for the Disciplinary Rules.
10056{_}3078{_}After starting from Ch'ang-gan, they passed through Lung, [3] and came to the kingdom of K'een-kwei,[4] where they stopped for the summer retreat.
10056{_}3079{_}When that was over, they went forward to the kingdom of Now-t'an, crossed the mountain of Yang-low, and reached the emporium of Chang-yih.
10056{_}3080{_}[5] There they found the country so much disturbed that travelling on the roads was impossible for them.
10056{_}3081{_}Its king, however, was very attentive to them, kept them in his capital, and acted the part of their danapati.
10056{_}3082{_}[6]  Here they met with Che-yen, Hwuy-keen, Sang-shao, Pao-yun, and Sang-king; and in pleasant association with them, as bound on the same journey with themselves, they passed the summer retreat of that year [7] together, resuming after it their travelling, and going on to T'un-hwang, [8] the chief town in the frontier territory of defence extending for about eighty li from east to west, and about forty from north to south.
10056{_}3083{_}Their company, increased as it had been, halted there for some days more than a month, after which Fa-hien and his four friends started first in the suite of an envoy, having separated for a time from Pao-yun and his associates.
10056{_}3084{_}Le Hao, the prefect of Tun-hwang, had supplied them with the means of crossing the desert before them, in which there are many evil demons and hot winds.
10056{_}3085{_}Travellers who encounter them perish all to a man.
10056{_}3086{_}There is not a bird to be seen in the air above, nor an animal on the ground below.
10056{_}3087{_}Though you look all round most earnestly to find where you can cross, you know not where to make your choice, the only mark and indication being the dry bones of the dead left upon the sand.
10056{_}3088{_}[Footnote 1: Ch'ang-gan is still the name of the principal district (and its city) in the department of Se-gan, Shen-se.
10056{_}3089{_}It had been the capital of the first empire of Han (B.C.
10056{_}3090{_}202 A.D. 24), as it subsequently was that of Suy (A.D.
10056{_}3091{_}589-618).]
10056{_}3092{_}[Footnote 2: The period Hwang-che embraced from A.D. 399 to 414, being the greater portion of the reign of Yao Hing of the After Ts'in, a powerful prince.
10056{_}3093{_}He adopted Hwang-che for the style of his reign in 399, and the cyclical name of that year was Kang-tsze.
10056{_}3094{_}It is not possible at this distance of time to explain, if it could be explained, how Fa-hien came to say that Ke-hae was the second year of the period.
10056{_}3095{_}It seems most reasonable to suppose that he set out on his pilgrimage in A.D. 399, the cycle name of which was Ke-hae.
10056{_}3096{_}In the "Memoirs of Eminent Monks" it is said that our author started in the third year of the period Lung-gan of the Eastern Ts'in, which was A.D.
10056{_}3097{_}399.]
10056{_}3098{_}[Footnote 3: Lung embraced the western part of Shen-se and the eastern part of Kan-suh.
10056{_}3099{_}The name remains in Lung Chow, in the extreme west of Shen-se.]
10056{_}3100{_}[Footnote 4: K'een-kwei was the second king of "the Western Ts'in."
10056{_}3101{_}Fa-hien would find him at his capital, somewhere in the present department of Lan-chow, Kan-suh.]
10056{_}3102{_}[Footnote 5: Chang-yih is still the name of a district in Kan-chow department, Kan-suh.
10056{_}3103{_}It is a long way north and west from Lan-chow, and not far from the Great Wall.
10056{_}3104{_}Its king at this time was, probably, Twan-yeh of "the northern Leang."]
10056{_}3105{_}[Footnote 6: Dana is the name for religious charity, the first of the six paramitas, or means of attaining to nirvana; and a danapati is "one who practises dana and thereby crosses the sea of misery."]
10056{_}3106{_}[Footnote 7: This was the second summer since the pilgrims left Ch'ang-gan.
10056{_}3107{_}We are now, therefore, probably, in A.D.
10056{_}3108{_}400.]
10056{_}3109{_}[Footnote 8: T'un-hwang is still the name of one of the two districts constituting the department of Gan-se, the most western of the prefectures of Kan-suh; beyond the termination of the Great Wall.]
10056{_}3110{_}CHAPTER II  ~On to Shen-shen and thence to Khoten~   After travelling for seventeen days, a distance we may calculate of about 1500 li, the pilgrims reached the kingdom of Shen-shen, a country rugged and hilly, with a thin and barren soil.
10056{_}3111{_}The clothes of the common people are coarse, and like those worn in our land of Han, [1] some wearing felt and others coarse serge or cloth of hair; this was the only difference seen among them.
10056{_}3112{_}The king professed our Law, and there might be in the country more than four thousand monks, who were all students of the hinayana.
10056{_}3113{_}[2] The common people of this and other kingdoms in that region, as well as the Sramans, [3] all practise the rules of India, only that the latter do so more exactly, and the former more loosely.
10056{_}3114{_}So the travellers found it in all the kingdoms through which they went on their way from this to the west, only that each had its own peculiar barbarous speech.
10056{_}3115{_}The monks, however, who had given up the worldly life and quitted their families, were all students of Indian books and the Indian language.
10056{_}3116{_}Here they stayed for about a month, and then proceeded on their journey, fifteen days' walking to the northwest bringing them to the country of Woo-e.
10056{_}3117{_}In this also there were more than four thousand monks, all students of the hinayana.
10056{_}3118{_}They were very strict in their rules, so that Sramans from the territory of Ts'in were all unprepared for their regulations.
10056{_}3119{_}Fa-hien, through the management of Foo Kung-sun, _maitre d'hotellerie_, was able to remain with his company in the monastery where they were received for more than two months, and here they were rejoined by Pao-yun and his friends.
10056{_}3120{_}At the end of that time the people of Woo-e neglected the duties of propriety and righteousness, and treated the strangers in so niggardly a manner that Che-yen, Hwuy-keen, and Hwuy-wei went back towards Kao-ch'ang, hoping to obtain there the means of continuing their journey.
10056{_}3121{_}Fa-hien and the rest, however, through the liberality of Foo Kung-sun, managed to go straight forward in a southwest direction.
10056{_}3122{_}They found the country uninhabited as they went along.
10056{_}3123{_}The difficulties which they encountered in crossing the streams and on their route, and the sufferings which they endured, were unparalleled in human experience, but in the course of a month and five days they succeeded in reaching Yu-teen.
10056{_}3124{_}[Footnote 1: This is the name which Fa-hien always uses when he would speak of China, his native country, as a whole, calling it from the great dynasty which had ruled it, first and last, for between four and five centuries.
10056{_}3125{_}Occasionally, as we shall immediately see, he speaks of "the territory of Ts'in or Ch'in," but intending thereby only the kingdom of Ts'in, having its capital in Ch'ang-gan.]
10056{_}3126{_}[Footnote 2: Meaning the "small vehicle, or conveyance."
10056{_}3127{_}There are in Buddhism the triyana, or "three different means of salvation, i.e.
10056{_}3128{_}of conveyance across the samsara, or sea of transmigration, to the shores of nirvana.
10056{_}3129{_}Afterwards the term was used to designate the different phases of development through which the Buddhist dogma passed, known as the mahayana, hinayana, and madhyamayana."
10056{_}3130{_}"The hinayana is the simplest vehicle of salvation, corresponding to the first of the three degrees of saintship."
10056{_}3131{_}E.H., pp.
10056{_}3132{_}151-2, 45, and 117.]
10056{_}3133{_}[Footnote 3: "Sraman" may in English take the place of Sramana, the name for Buddhist monks, as those who have separated themselves from (left) their families, and quieted their hearts from all intrusion of desire and lust.]
10056{_}3134{_}CHAPTER III  ~Khoten--Processions of Images~   Yu-Teen is a pleasant and prosperous kingdom, with a numerous and flourishing population.
10056{_}3135{_}The inhabitants all profess our Law, and join together in its religious music for their enjoyment.
10056{_}3136{_}The monks amount to several myriads, most of whom are students of the mahayana.
10056{_}3137{_}[1] They all receive their food from the common store.
10056{_}3138{_}Throughout the country the houses of the people stand apart like separate stars, and each family has a small tope [2] reared in front of its door.
10056{_}3139{_}The smallest of these may be twenty cubits high, or rather more.
10056{_}3140{_}They make in the monasteries rooms for monks from all quarters, the use of which is given to travelling monks who may arrive, and who are provided with whatever else they require.
10056{_}3141{_}The lord of the country lodged Fa-hien and the others comfortably, and supplied their wants, in a monastery called Gomati, of the mahayana school.
10056{_}3142{_}Attached to it there are three thousand monks, who are called to their meals by the sound of a bell.
10056{_}3143{_}When they enter the refectory, their demeanor is marked by a reverent gravity, and they take their seats in regular order, all maintaining a perfect silence.
10056{_}3144{_}No sound is heard from their alms-bowls and other utensils.
10056{_}3145{_}When any of these pure men require food, they are not allowed to call out to the attendants for it, but only make signs with their hands.
10056{_}3146{_}Hwuy-king, Tao-ching, and Hwuy-tah set out in advance towards the country of K'eeh-ch'a; but Fa-hien and the others, wishing to see the procession of images, remained behind for three months.
10056{_}3147{_}There are in this country four great monasteries, not counting the smaller ones.
10056{_}3148{_}Beginning on the first day of the fourth month, they sweep and water the streets inside the city, making a grand display in the lanes and byways.
10056{_}3149{_}Over the city gate they pitch a large tent, grandly adorned in all possible ways, in which the king and queen, with their ladies brilliantly arrayed, take up their residence for the time.
10056{_}3150{_}The monks of the Gomati monastery, being mahayana students, and held in greatest reverence by the king, took precedence of all the others in the procession.
